SPANIARDS ARE TREADING
ON TREACHEROUS GROUND
WASHINGTON, Nov. 15.âAdminis
tration officials do not share the opin
ion expressed in some quarters that the
Paris Peace Commission will fail in its
efforts to agree upon a treaty. This
opinion is not based upon any specific
information received from Paris, but
rather upon the fact that by withdraw
ing from the conference Spain has
u>>thing to gain and all to lose.
It is undoubtedly true that should
the Spaniards adopt this course they
â would sacrifice ttie indemnity this Gov
ernment is now disposed to grant, and
would also precipitate trouble for
themselves all along the line. The
United States undoubtedly would im
mediately take forcible possession of
the entire Philippine group and at
once enter upon a military and naval
campaign, which would end only upon
an unconditional surrender of what
ever territory this Government might
see fit to demand.
It is believed that the United States
at this time would consent to a cash
payment of from $20,000,000 to $25,000,
000 rather than renew the war, but the
longer a final settlement is delayed tha
smaller will be the indemnity. In any
event, the President is not disposed to
brook unnecessary prolongation of the
negotiations, and it is believed has so
Indira teil to our Peace Commissioners.
SYNDICATE SCHEME VIEWED
AS A SPANISH RUSE
WASHINGTON, Nov. 15.â Inquiry
has been made int<> the character of the
projectors of the remarkable syndicate
scheme for the purchase of the Philip
pines for the sum of $400,000,000. The
result has been to show that they are
irresponsible parties and it is scarcely
to be doubted that the project has been
put forward as a means of enhancing
the price which the Spaniards may de
mand for the relinquishment of the
Philippines. In other words, the Span
iards might claim justification for de
manding a sum as large as $200,000,000
if they could point to the fact that a
syndicate stood ready to pay double
that price for the islands.

WASHINGTON, Nov. 15.â The Gov
ernment has renewed and strength
ened its determination that the Span
ish evacuation of Cuba and the Ameri

MARTINELLI HAS NOT
HEARD OF THE DECREE
Knows Nothing of the Reported Ac
tion of the Pope Regarding
Burial of Masons.
NEW YORK, Nov. 15.â The "Washing
ton correspondent of the Herald tele
graphs: The report that Aigr. Marti
nellf. papal delegate In this city, had
been Informed that the Pope had placed
# ban on Father Zurcher's book and had
Special Dispatch to The CaH
I issued a decree permitting priests to
bury Mason?, was called to the attention
of Pr. Rooker, Secretary of the Legation,
this evening.
"We have received no information about
these reports," said Dr. Rooker. "All we
know about them is what we have seen
in the newspapers. Even if Father Zur
chcr's book had been condemned, we
would have no official knowledge about it.
In such case Fatber Zurcher would be no
tified of the papal decree. I believe a
priest whose book is condemned is given
n year in which to submit whatever he
wlsnes to say about it and to destroy
copies of the book he is able to secure
from circulation. A priest, before print
ing a book, must submit it to the bishop
of the diocese in which it is to be printed
and must have the imprimature of the
bishop.
"So far as the decree regarding the
burial of Masons is concerned, we know
nothing of it. No Catholic can be a
Freemason, and the church Is not in the
habit of burying people who are not Cath
olics."
SCHLEY IS IMPRESSED
WITH PORTO RICO
Rear Admiral Thinks the Future Has
Great Things in Store for
the Island.
"WASHINGTON. Nov. 15. â Admiral
Sehley. who Is in Washington, said to The
Call correspondent to-day:
"I. am greatly impressed with Porto
Rico. I think it has. under the domina
tion of the I'nited States, a brilliant fu
ture. Of course the industries of the isl
and are not developed as fully as they
would be under our system of govern
ment and with our energy. It will be nec
essary first to educate the people to the
new regime, for the reason that 400 years
of Spanish domination have established,
not only the customs, but the language
and methods of the political government,
which are largely at variance with our
system of government. It is the contrast
of day and night. Much Is to be said,
however, in favor of the people of Porto
Rico. They are peaceable and gentle and
entirely unaccustomed to war, and I think
could be easily molded to our way of do
ing things. There was a very marked
feeling of gratification plainly visible
among the native Porto Ricans at the
evacuation of the Spanish army.
"I think Porto Ricans, having enjoyed
a longer period of freedom from devastat
ing wars, are in much better condition for
progress than the Cubans. The former
will yield much more readily to the new
order of things than the latter," conclud
ed the Admiral.

BRECKINRIDGE'S
LOW ESTIMATE
OF SHAFTER
Intimated His Superior's Talents
Were Inadequate to the
Situation.
Declared That Results Before Santiago Would
Have Been Different if General Miles
WASHINGTON, Nov. 15. â Gen
eral Breckinridge's testimony to
day dealt with the campaign in Cuba
and the transportation of troops to that
island. He had, he said, gone to Cuba
as inspector general. Arriving at
Tampa on the sth of June, he had ob
served casually the preparations for the
disembarkation of troops before the ex
pedition left and had concluded that
they were quite inadequate.
"I made no report, however," he said,
"as General Miles was on the ground
and could observe for himself."
He was questioned at some length
concerning the conduct of the cam
i paign in front of Santiago, and replied
that the result was what he had ex
pected it would be. Everything there,
| he said, was strained to get men on
board, and, consequently, there was
comparatively little effort to secure
! equipment. While he knew nothing
: definitely of the plans of the campaign,
he was sure there was some plan. He
| was quite certain, from a conversation
i he had with General Miles, that he had
] a plan, though he did not say that this
| was the plan carried into effect.
"Of course," suggested Governor
! Beaver, "Santiago was the goal of the
j campaign?"
"Certainly."
Asked as to his opinion of the result
of the campaign, he said he thought it
was consistent with the character of
the men who conducted it â what was to
1 be expected from the limitations of
General Shatter.
"I think it would have been differ
ent," he said, "if General Miles had
been in commandâ ffcat it would have
been more satisfactorily conducted. I
think he has more of the spark and
genius of command than any man in
the army, and I am sure that if he had
had charge he would have been in the
front rank."
Ex-Governor Woodbury suggested
that the battle in front of San Juan
had been fought and the victory won
within eight days of landing.
General Breckinridge in reply said
the victory had been won "when Gen
eral Toral's nerve gave out." This, he
continued, had been the war of all
others when success had been won in
its earliest stages. He believed that
the war was the Lord's war, and that
this wonderful success was due to his
supervision. The time for the battle
was, however, short enough. Yet, it
must be remembered that the distance
between the battlefield and Sibony was
less than half a day's march, but, while
he considered the result as marvelous
ly commendable, he did not feel that
General Shafter was above criticism in
the conduct of the campaign. He could
not believe the result was a miracle,
but he ascribed the outcome to the fact
that the ai.ny was one which was ca
pable of meeting all calls.
"No matter how it was tangled up,"
he said, "it went to victory."
He had at the time quoted Lord
Beresford, when he said to his army
upon a certain occasion:
"I have led you into a devil of a fix,
and it is now your duty to fight like
hell to get me out of it."
He was sure, at least, that all en
SAN FRANCISCO, WEDNESDAY, NOVEMBER 16, 1898
MAJOR GENERAL JOSEPH C. BRECKJNRIDGE.
Had Commanded.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
General McCook asked General
Breckinridge if he had visited General
Shafter in his tent when the latter was
ill at Santiago, and General Breckin
ridge replied that he had done so.
"Did he then offer the command of
the army to you, as has been reported
in the press?"
General Breckinridge replied:
â  "I very much prefer not to answer
that question. You should ask General
Shafter. Such a conversation as oc
curred between General Shafter and
myself on that occasion is not of a
character to be spoken of lightly. I
fail to see how a reply from me would
promote the object of your inquiry. You
want to know what General Shafter
did, not what he may have thought of
doing. If General Shafter has not re
peated the conversation, I ought not to
do so."
"As a matter of fact, he did not turn
the command over to you?" suggested
Governor Beaver, \o which General
Breckinridge responded:
"No, he did not, and I do not think
there is any impropriety in my saying
that he could not have done so."
With this the matter was allowed to
drop and General Breckinridge was ex
cused.
Dr. William G. Weaver of the Ninth
Pennsylvania Volunteers proved an im
portant witness. Dr. Weaver said that
typhoid had broken out about the 18th
of June, and that it had increased from
that time until the 20th of August.
There had been about 600 cases of this
disease in^the regiment and twenty
eight or thirty deaths. The doctor
said there was an insufficient supply of
drugs, and that at times he failed to
get what was needed. He said that
at one time there were flfty-six typhoid j
patients in the regimental hospital, be
cause there was no accomodation for
them in the division hospital. At an
other time there were sixty-seven fever
patients in the company tents, for
whom admission could not be secured
in any hospital, division or regimental.
In summing up the causes of dis
eases in the camp, Dr. Weaver includ
ed overwork of the men as one. He
also expressed the opinion that the
water from Chickamauga Creek was
polluted. He considered the water in
this stream as hardly fit to bathe in,
much less to drink.
gaged in the war had acted with zeal,
and every man had done the best of
which he was capable, and he had
never known a war in which there was
not criticism at the close of it.
Resuming his remarks of a general
character, General Breckinridge ex
pressed the opinion that there had not
been enough fighting to obscure other
results common to war. He thought
that the evils which had been experi
enced were inevitable under our Ameri
can system. He condemned this system
as most reprehensible and said the vol
unteer soldier must always suffer the
worst of the consequences.
"If the regular stands up under it bet
ter than the volunteer, it is only be
cause he has had time to adjust him
self to the unfavorable conditions."
He traced all the trouble to the want
of preparation and mobilization in time
of peace and that, in turn, to the failure
of Congress to appropriate the neces
sary money for reforms.
Replying to a question from Mr.
Woodbury, he said that, considering
the system, he thought the Government
"has done far better in its conduct of
the war than anybody had expected,
and just about one-fourth as well as
it should have done."
FORTHCOMING
MESSAGE OF
MR. McKINLEY
Much Will Be Left to
Congress.
RECOMMENDATIONS ARE FEW
NO STATED POLICY AS TO NEW
ACQUISITIONS.
Will Favor a Large Increase in the
Navy and the Revival of the
Grade of Admiral for
I>ewey.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Nov. 15.â The Herald's
Washington correspondent sends the
following: Those who have been ex
pecting President McKinley to make
specific recommendations in his annual
message to Congress as to the future
form of government of the territory
wrested from Spain will probably be
disappointed.
The message, now well under way,
will, I understand, point out that it is
too early yet to reach a decision in this
matter. The President appreciates that
Congress will desire to have a voice in
I shaping the policy in regard to the fu
| ture control of the Philippines and
! Porto Rico; that congressional com
j missions will probably be appointed to
; visit these new possessions and to in
vestigate and report upon the condi
tions in each island, and he is perfectly
willing that ample time should be al
lowed for all this to be done. It is his
purpose to have the conquered islands
continued under strong military gov
ernment pending a definite conclusion
by the executive and Congress as to the
future of these islands. He considers
it will take at least a year to enable
the American people to determine what
our relations should be toward the new
SITE OF THE NEW TERMINAL, OF THE VALLEY ROAD.
possessions. That the Islands may be
kept out of politics as much as possi
ble it is his purpose to have all islands
governed by military forces, and with
this idea in view army officers will be
assigned to perform all civil as well
as military functions.
The President will not recommend
any legislation affecting the tariff and
currency systems of Cuba, Porto Rico
or the Philippines. It is the present
purpose of the administration to allow
these recent possessions of Spain to re
main under military control for at least
a year, during which time the relations
which the islands are to bear to the
United States can be settled and all
their needs and conditions carefully
considered.
It is said that in the meantime there
will be no changes in regard to cur
rency or tariffs, for while the newly
acquired territor"- is under military
control, the President, as commander
in-chief of the army and navy, is an
absolute autocrat, and by military or
ders can institute new financial and
revenue systems at will. By this course
the administration will keep the sub
ject of currency and bank reform be
fore the country until the first meet
ing of the Fifty-sixth Congress, with
its Republican majority in both houses,
when the task of correcting the evils
of the present monetary system will be
taken up in earnest.
Revenfle revision is not to be recom
mended to Congress by the President
or the Secretary of the Treasury. The
expenditures of the Government are
now far in excess of the receipts, and
will probably continue to be so for some
time even after the formal conclusion
of peace with Spain. Secretary Gage
will advise some minor amendments to
the war revenue law, chiefly of an ad
ministrative character, but the whole
subject of revision of the currency to
conform to the changed situation of the
country will be left for the next Con
gress, when it will be more definitely
known what the situation will be.
The army reorganization will con
template at least 100,000 men. There
is no reason to believe that any recom
mendation will be made as to a dif
ferent basis of organization, that in
force now being considered compact
and complete and satisfactory. But a
departure will be made as relates to
what is recommended touching the
army in Cuba and the use to be made
of the native army, with a gradual ;id
mixture of Cuban troops with United
States troops. This will involve, ac
cording to the recommendation, the or
ganization of a regiment on the island
on a basis at first of one battalion of
Cuban troops to two battalions cf

the approaching twenty-round bout between Jim Corbett and Tom
Sharkey at the Lenox Athletic Club on November 22 show what a
great amount of interest is b^ing taken in this battle. While the
majority of those interviewed so far favor Corbett to beat the sailor,
the latter is constantly gaining friends. The opinions gathered are
as follows:
Kid McCoyâ The rules under which the men are to box prohibit
hitting in the clinches and on breakaways, which gives Corbett a de
cided advantage. I therefore think he will get the decision and the
bout will probably go the limit.
Tom O'Rotirkeâ l think the bout will go the limit and Sharkey
will get the decision.
George Considine â Corbett will surely win inside of fifteen rounds.
Charley "Whiteâ l cannot sop how Corbett can lose. He should
beat the sailor in seven or eight rounds.
Billy Gray â If Sharkey keeps his head and does not lose on a
foul he ought to win inside of nine rounds.
Sam Austinâ l think it will be an exceptionally clever fight and
if Corbett can stand off the sailor's rushes for twenty rounds I think
he will win.
Billy Newman â Corbett, on account of his great cleverness, should

CHAMBERLAIN
TALKS PLAINLY
AS TO FRANCE
Republic Must Alter
Its Policy.
SPEECH CONVEYS A THREAT
TOO MUCH INTERFERENCE WITH
ENGLISH AFFAIRS.
Secretary of the Colonies Pays a
Warm 'xTibute to America,
Which Is Received
With Cheers.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANCHESTER. Nov. 15.â At the Na
.ional Liberal Unionists' conference
here to-day, Joseph Chamberlain, Sec
retary of State for the Colonies, made
his first public speech since his return
from the United States.
Mr. Chamberlain, who was much
more explicit on the subject of Anglo-
French relations than any of his col
leagues in the Cabinet has been, said
it was the hope of every friend of peace
that the French withdrawal from Fa
ehodu was indicative of their accept
ance of the principle of British control
of the whole valley of the Nile, "re
garding which there cannot be any dis
cussion whatever."
Going on to recite a "series of un
friendly acts perpetrated by Franca in
other quarters of the globe during the
last ten or fifteen years," he said:
"If better relations are to be estab
lished it vfill be necessary for French
politicians to abandon tactics whose
object has been to embarrass British
policies in quarters where the French
have no interests to protect. I refer es
pecially to Newfoundland, where, de
spite the fact that the French fishery
Interests have declined to a compara
tively insignificant point, the demands
of the French have continually in
creased, and their interference with the
development of this colony has im
creased.
"At the present moment Newfound
land is seriously suffering from an in
tervention which is of no advantage to
France, although a serious detriment
to the British colony. If the Fashoda
Incident only serves to disabuse foreign
Etatesmen of the erroneous conception
that the British will yield anything to
pressure, it will be a blessing in dis
guise."
After referring to the "thorough and
complete sympathy so noticeable be
tween the mother country and her col
onies," Mr. Chamberlain continued as
follows:
"What is of equal consequence, our
American kin (loud cheers) have begun
to understand us better. If we have
had differences in Ihe past, I believe
they have arisen entirely from the want
of proper mutual understanding, but
now the Americans know that in the
late irouble our hearts went out to
them (cheers), and they heartily re-
Diprocate our feelings.
"I shall not attempt to predict what
may follow this better feeling, but I
may at least hope that in the future
the understanding of which we have
spoken may be perfected, and that in
the face of that understanding we two
may be able to guarantee peace and
civilization to the world." (Prolonged
cheers.)

SUPREME COURT
REARS TWO CASES
Murderer Hoff's Appeal
Argued.
TAKEN UNDER ADVISEMENT
LOS ANGELES RATE DECISION
CONSIDERED.
Conflicting Claims of the City and
the Monopoly Presented by
Senator White and J.
S. Chapman.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SACRAMENTO, Nov. 15.â Before the
Supreme Court to-day motions were
heard and argued in two important
cases. One was that of George Hoff,
undtr sentence of death for the murder
of Mrs. Mary E. Clute in Ban Francisco
on December 15 last year. The other
was in the case in which the city cor
poration of Los Angeles and the water
works company of that place are in
volved.
The motion in tae case of -ioff was
presented and argued by William H.
Schooler on behalf of the appellant and
Deputy Attorney General C. N. Post on
behalf of the State. Schooler sought to
show that Judge Carroll Cook had
erred in rejecting certain instructions
which the attorney desired to have read
to the jury. Schooler argued that the
instructions were a copy of a decision
rendered by Justice Baldwin and con
curred in by Stephen J. Field when the
latter was Chief Justice of this State.
The instruction related to the subject
of motive. Hoff was sentenced on April
5 to be hanged June 10, but the appeal
which was argued to-day operated to
arrest execution of the death sentence.
It is now believed that Hoff, even if the
Supreme Court refuses to order a new
trial, will not be resentenced until next
spring, since it will be some months
before the papers in the present matter
are handed down.
Senator Stephen M. White and J. S.
Chapman of Los Angeles appeared in
the water case, known as the Los An
geles rate case. The case deals with
conflicting claims of the city and the
Los Angeles Water Company relative
to the right to collect charges for
water from the citizens of Los Angeles
and relative also to the power of the
lower court to appoint a receiver. The
sum of $27,000 a month represents the
income affected by the litigation. The
company claims that the city cannot
take possession of the water works un
til payment has been made for the im
provements which have been construct
ed by the company since 1868. The
lower court decided that the ~ity could
not take possession without paying for
the works, but nevertheless appointed
a receiver. The city is now appealing
from the former feature of the decision
and the company from the latter.
John Scott and City Attorney Dunn
conducted the arguments on behalf of
the city. The matter was taken under
advisement by the court.
MRS. STEVENS HONORED.
Elected President of the Woman's
Christian Temperance Union.
ST. PAUL, Minn., Nov. 15.â The storm
of yesterday in the Woman's Christian
Temperance Union convention caused a
small attendance at the convention to
day. The election of officers was pre
ceded by the singing of "Guide Me, Oh
Thy Great Jehovah." Mrs. Stevens was
elected president On the first ballot, and
made a touching speech of acceptance,
urging all to join in carrying on the work
to which Miss Willard, the beloved lead
er, had given her life. The Maine dele
gation presented her with a beautiful
cluster of roses.
Mrs. Stevens then stated she wanted to
nominate Miss Anna A. Gordon, who had
been Miss Willard's private secretary,
and is probably one or the most beloved
of the union leaders, to be vice-president
at large. The by-laws were then amend
ed to give the president the authority to
make such nomination, and it was im
mediately made, the election following by
a rising unanimous vote.
For corresponding secretary Mrs. S. H.
Frey of Minnesota received 204 votes out
of 353. Mra. Clara C. Hoffman of Mis
souri wa? re-elected recording secretary.
Mrs. Frances E. Beauchamp of Ken
tucky wa - made assistant recording sec
retary. T.Â». ââ¢ ballot for treasurer resulted:
Mrs. Helf>n M. Barker, 285; Mrs. M. H.
Dunham, 41; scattering, 20.
The Temple matter came up again in
the convention this afternoon and a reso
lution indorsing 1 the Temple was defeated
by a vote of 76 to 277.
GILROY'S POSTMASTER WEDS.
Richard M. Ryan and Miss Kate
Schafer United.
GILROY. Nov. 15.â The marriage of
Richard M. Ryan, Gilroy's popular post
master, and Miss Kate Schafer, the
charming daughter of Mr. and Mrs.
Henry Schafer, was solemnized to-day at
St Mary's Catholic Church. A high
nuptial mass was celebrated by Rev.
Father Hudson. The church was a
bower of smilax, ferns, ivy and potted
flowers and plants. The sacred edifice
was filled with relatives and friends of
the couple. After the ceremony a wed
ding breakfast was partaken of. Mr. and
Mrs. Ryan left for Ban Francisco this
afternoon.

HARES GIVE THEIR
FOES HARD RUNS
Exciting- Coursing Near
Merced.
ALL CONDITIONS FAVORABLE
WEATHER FAIR AND THE GAME
FULL OF LIFE.
Besults of the First Round of the All-
Aged Stake, and How the Con
tests Were Lost and
Won.
BY M. J GEARY.
MERCED, Nov. 15.â The weather for
the inaugural day's meeting of the
Ingleside Coursing Club on the far
famed plains of Merced was cool and
decidedly enjoyable. The attendance
was very large â in fact teams sufficient
to accommodate the seekers of first
class sport could not be obtained at any
price at 7 o'clock this morning. The
start from Merced to the happy cours
ing grounds was made soon after the
innner man was refreshed by a good
morning's breakfast, and, although the
roads leading to the feeding and play
ing fields of the highly conditioned
jacks were dusty in places, the drive to
and from the coursing was enjoyed by
all.
The coursing commenced immediately
after the president of the club had ad
dressed the meeting 1 , and read parts of
the riot act to the unruly and ungov
ernable. Hares of the "never known to
quit when running" variety were found
ad libitum, and as a consequence there
are many quiet and docile dogs housed
in their kennels as I write. Courses of
from two to five miles were many, and
in most instances the hares showed
considerable disrespect for the dogs by
parting company with them at times
when fur was muchly needed. The
ground coursed over was of short stub
ble â sandy surface that is not desirable
for coursing, although a good shower
would have made it admirable for the
sport. The game was so strong of foot
and limb that in most instances the
d"gs only scored wrenches until they
had got well out of sight of the specta
tors.
It must be remembered that ihe de
scriptive account of each course which
follows is given from an observer's
point of view; i. c., while dogs and hare
were in sight of those who sat in ve
hicles. Of course, after they had passed
out of sight the report ended, although
the scores were kindly given by Judge
Grace through his field assistant, T. J.
MoHugh.

TRAINED NURSES FOR MANILA. ,
All That Can Be Secured to Be Sent
Immediately.
PORTLAND, Or., Nov. 15.â Miss Abbie
H. Zaner, directress of nurses at Portland
hospital, today received a dispatch from
Surgeon General Sternbergf of the United
States army, to proceed to Manila im
mediately with as many trained nurses as
could be secured in Portland. Miss Zaner
will leave for San Francisco to-morrow
and will be accompanied by the following
trained nurses: Miss Josephine Dunn,
Miss Cecelia Earhart and Mab*l J. Lake.
They will probably sail for Manila Fri
day.
LI PLEADS IN VAIN.
PEKING, Nov. 15.â Li Hung Chang has
memorialized the Dowager Empress, beg
ging to be excused from the appointment
to consult with the viceroy of the pro
vince of Shan Tung as to the steps to be
taken to prevent any further overflowing
of the Yellow River, pleading age and In
firmity as his excuse. He had a special
audience on the subject with the Dowager
Empress to-day and the latter, while
thanking him for hlB service to the
throne, requested the Chinese statesman
to proceed to the Yellow without delay.

BATTLE BETWEEN
IMMUNES AND
CUBAN POLICE
Six Lives Lost in the
Conflict.
SOLDIERS RESISTED ARREST
WERE DRUNK AND ATTEMPTED
TO STEAL A HOG.
General Wood Visits San Luis, the
Scene of the Slaughter, and
Will Promptly Mete Out
Punishment.
Special Cable to The Call and the New York
Herald. Copyrighted, 1898, by James Gor
don Bennett.
SANTIAGO, Nov. 15.â San Luis was
the scene last night of a fatal encoun
ter between rural guards and intoxi
cated soldiers of the Ninth Immunes,
Twenty-third Kansas and Eighth Illi
nois, in which Sergeant Rafael Ferer,
chief of the Provisional Mounted Po
lice, one of the guards, one negro of
the Ninth Immunes, Jose Ramon; a
planter, Emilio Bettern, and a child
were killed.
the disturbance was caused by an at
tempt of the soldiers to steal a hog be
longing to the Cubans, who protested.
It was pay day and a large number of
soldiers were intoxicated, all joining in
the fight.
Sergeant Ferer and his men, in reply
to a call, soon reached the house where
the thieves were, but they escaped and
were joined by twenty companions
armed with Springfield rifles taken
from camp contrary to orders. The
fight began in a house near the Norma
estate, continuing on the way toward
the house of the manager of the es
tate.
Ferer's companions barricaded them
selves while Ferer remained outside ex
postulating with the negroes and try
ing to persuade them to desist. He
was shot through the head, dying after
staggering to the door and ordering his
men to defend themselves and the lives
of the people on the estate. The police
fired from cover, killing one soldier and
wounding several. Two of the police
men were shot while attempting to es
cape. Ramon and Battern were shot
while trying to disperse the soldiers.
The officers of the regiments deny that
their soldiers did the shooting, against
incontrovertible evidence to the con
trary.
General Wood and Captain Beacon
visited San Luis this morning to
investigate. They found the wounds
were made by 45-caliber bullets. They
acted promptly, offering a reward of
$1000 in gold for disclosure of the iden
tity of the men implicated in the shoot
ing. General Wood returned to-day,
cabling the details to Washington to
night, with recommendations that im
mediate punishment should follow.

at St. Louis â Pope Cables His

ST. LOUIS, Nov. 15.â Miss Kathryn

which was the most brilliant of the sea
son, was a semi-military affair. Lieuten
ant McKenna with four of his grooms
men, appearing in full dress uniform.
Many persons were present.
Archbishop Kaine of St. Louis, assisted
by Bishop Donahue of West Virginia, per
formed the marriage ceremony, which
was held in St. Alphonsus Church.

Ireland of St. Paul, Bishop Watterson of
Columbus, Rev. Thomas Hendrick of Buf
falo and all the prfests of St. Alphonsus

away and the ceremony was made the
most impressive by the fact that the
benediction of Pope Leo was this morn
ing cabled to the bridal couple. After
the ceremony at the church a reception
was held at the Kerens residence in Van
deventer place.
Richard Kerens Jr. acted as Best man
and the groomsmen were Lieutenants
Wylde, Winn and Shields of the Twelfth
United States Infantry, Lieutenant Gregg
of the Fourth Cavalry, and Edgar Lack
land of St. Louis. Miss Gladys Kerens
acted as maid of honor for her sister and
the bridesmaids were Miss- McKenna and
Miss Marie McKenna of Washington,
Miss Mary Soper and Miss Jane Henry
of New York and Miss Sadie Pierce of
St. Louis.

NEW YORK, Nov. 15.-L. J. Morrison, counsel for'the pro
tective committee of the English Central Pacific stock
holders, announced to-day that he had received a cablegram
from London to the effect that the English committee,
through one of its members, had commenced a representa
tive action to restrain the board of directors of the Central
Pacific Railway Company from putting a new mortgage on
the property to take up the Government loan and the pres
ent mortgage.
COLUMBIA GOES
TO THE BOTTOM
Seattle-HonoJulu Liner
Sinks at Hilo.
PASSENGERS AND CREW SAVED
SERIES OF THBILLIxVG MIS
FORTUNES.
Tossed by a Furious Gale for Two
Days and Nights, Her Back
** Broken and Hold Filled
With. Water.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
PORT TOWNSEND, Wash., Nov. 15.
The steamship Centennial of the Se- ;
attle and Honolulu line arrived this
morning, bringing additional details of
the misfortunes of the steamship
Columbia of the same line. The Colum- '
bia was placed on the Seattle-Honolulu â 
route and on her arrival at Honolulu j
she was covered with libel suits. After i
much delay the libels were temporarily
Batisfied and the vessel started on the
return voyage October 29. From the i
time she left port defects were being
found among various parts of her ma- \
chlnery which kept the engineering de- !
partment busy fixing them, and the !
pumps commenced giving uut one at a
time. To add to these misfortunes a
â  heavy northeast wind sprung up, the j
vessel rolled and pitched and the pas- j
eengers were tossed about like leaves, j
it being impossible to walk either on
deck or in the cabin. On November 31 j
the gale increased and it was found j
that a seam had opened amidships, that \
the steam pipes were straining badly I
end the vessel was being rilled with es- j
caping steam from the joints.
Investigation showed that water was
streaming in over the cement places on
the sides of the room opposite the en
gines and that the boilers had broken
loose from their cement foundations, j
Further Investigation revealed the fact j
that seven streams from six to eight j
inches wide and one inch in thickness
were pouring- in amidships and another
stream twelve inches wide was pouring
into the lire room. The condition of the |
vessel became critical and Captain 1
Milnor called the officers together for
consultation. Running for various
points was discussed and it was de
cided to make for Hiio. The vessel was
In latitude 26 degrees 14 minutes north,
400 miles from Hilo, when the order was
given to put about.
When the vessel turned the steam
steering gear broke and hand gear was
coupled on. The storm increased in
fury, two more seams opened in the
stern, and the one amidships was con
stantly growing larger. Another also
opened in the sleeve of the stern gear-
Ing through which the water was rush- j
Ing. The shaft had been knocked out
of place and was turning at an angle,
causing a friction which Â»heated the
â water coming through that part of the
vessel. The water-tight bulkheads in
the lower Hold separating the boiler and
engine-rooms from the fore and aft
compartments were torn loose. These
spaces would open and close with the
rolling of the vessel. So heavily did the
vessel labor that her backbone parted
amidships and it became h fight for life.
Boats were got in readiness for im
mediate use, and according to Captain
Milnor it seemed impossible to reach
port. Great coolness was displayed in
the engineers' de] ..rtm^nt, where the
bursting of the main steam pipe was
momentarily expected, which meant
death to all therein.
The Columbia finally reached Hllo in j
a sinking condition and the passengers
and crew were landed while the vessel
was gradually going to the bottom.
Several of the crew were severely in- |
jured. The passengers were transferred
to the Centennial and safely landed I
here. Captain Milnor has asked for a
survey of the steamer by the board of j
underwriters. The Columbia has had
bad luck ever since her advent on this
coast. She received injuries on the
South American coast, requiring ex
tensive repairs, which were not proper
ly made, I^aks being plastered up with
cement, and paintr-d so it was not no
ticeable to the underwriters' inspectors.
The vessel had been condemned by
the board of survey. She was valued
at about $100,000. was insured for $60,000,
and was owned principally by Alex
ander Baillie of Tacoma.

REVOLUTION IN
SAN SALVADOR
President Gutierrez Is a
Prisoner.
CAPTURED BY THE REBELS
BEVOLT MAY ASSUME SOME IM
PORTANCE.
Is Not Directed Against the Chief
Executive, but Against the
Confederation of Re
publics.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, Nov. 15.â The Wash
ington correspondent of the Herald tel
egraphs: A cablegram received at the
State Department from Acting Consul
Baruch at San Salvador confirms the
announcement of a revolution in Sal
vador. The message further states that
President Gutierrez has been taken
prisoner. No request for a warship is
made, but in view of the seriousness of
the situation it is probable that the
orders to the Philadelphia will be
changed so that she will start as soon
as possible for the Salvadorean coast,
or instructions will be given to the gun
boat \v heeling to proceed to La Liber
tad and provide protection for Ameri
can interests.
With the capture of the president of
the state, the revolution In Central
America usually ends, but this is not
likely to be th^ case, as Salvador is a
state in the Greater Republic of Cen
tral America, and Nicaragua and Hon
duras are required by the constitution
thereof to put down the rebellion. They
will probably restore Senor Gutierrez
to power. A diplomat familiar with the
situation in Central Ajnerica told me
to-night that the revolt was not par
ticularly directed against Senor Gutier
rez, but against the Greater Republic.
"There's a conservative party in Sal- j
vador," he said, "which is opposed to
the union and has taken advantage of
the fact that the republic is only two
weeks old to bring about a revolution
in order to discredit It in the eyes of
the world. President Gutierrez was a
conservative until recently and opposed
the entrance of Salvador into the union,
but the reports made by Salvadorean I
delegates to Congress showt I the ad- â 
vantages of the union and he changed
his ideas. Salvador is now like one of
the States of the United States. She
Is responsible to the federal govern
ment and this government will sup
press the revolution. Strong bodies of
troops can be sent into Salvador with
dispatch by Honduras and these
troops can be quickly reinforced from
Nicaragua.
OUTLAWS MEET
HOT RESISTANCE
Three Slain and Four
Wounded.
Special Dispatoh to The Call.
NEW YORK, Nov. 15.â A Sun spe
cial from Wichita, Kans., says: Three
dead and four wounded outlaws are in
a, back room of the Chelsea National
Bank at Chelsea, Indian Territory, to
night, the victims of a fight with Dep
uty Marshals in the streets of that
town early this morning. Two Deputy
Marshals and one citizen are seriously
injured. The dead are:
Zip Williams.
J. Harry Williams.
John Uourd.
All are outlaws. The wounded are:
Cook Still, outlaw, shot through the
right sh'nildor.
Sam Sixkiller, outlaw, shot in leg.
Harry King, outlaw, both arms shot
through.
Red Bird, full-blood Creek Indian,
shot in breast; will die.
Deputy Marshals Hendrix and James
were both shot in breast, and Will Par
ker, a citizen, was hit in the arm by a
stray bullet.
The battle began at daylight when
the train came in and was waged for a
half-hour. The outlaws came into
Chelsea about 3 o'clock In the morning.
Intending to rob the bank before any
one was awake and then hold up the
passenger train which arrives at Chel
sea at daylight.
Their plan to rob the bank succeeded,
but when they rode over to stop the
passenger train the outlaws were met
vith a heavy fire from police aboard
the train.
When the train came in the two Wil
liams boys and John Gourd jumped on
tht first passenger coach and started
through, while the others tackled the
express car.
Deputy Marshals and a posse of eight
men were in the smoker and opened
fire, killing the three outlaws who came
into the coach before the outlaws had a
chance to fire a shot.
The others started toward their po
nies. All the Marshals remained on
the coach, except Hendrix and James,
who jumped out and started to fol
low.
The four outlaws took refuge behind
their ponies and a battle waged for a
half hour. Finally all the outlaws
were so badly injured that they -
forced to surrender.
Accused of Setting Forest Fires.
LOS ANGELES, Nov. 15.â Deputy Unit
ed States Marshal Pourade brought in
from San Jacinto to-day Christ Mo Kane
and William Harley, two sheep herders
who are charged with mallcioussy setting
fire on the Government forest reservation.
They were examined and their trial set
for November 21. Charles Benris was
brought from San Bernardino, charged
with the same offense, except that he was
charged with carelessness instead of ma
liciousness
THE SAN FEANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, NOVEMBER 16, 1898.
SECRETARY OF STATE-ELECT.
CHARLES F- CURRY.
BRYAN ON THE
LATE ELECTION
Not a Sweeping Repub
lican Victory.
GAINS EQUALED BY LOSSES
QUESTIONS OVERSHADOWED BY
THE WAR.
Free Silver Coinage, He Says, Will
Again Be the Paramount Issue
After the Conclusion
of Peace.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
LINCOLN, Neb., Nov. 15.â Colonel W.
J. Bryan to-night gave to the Asso
ciated Press the following interview
relative to the late election:
"While I do not understand that ser
vice in the volunteer army prevents a
soldier from expressing opinions upon
political questions, I declined to take
part in the late campaign lest I should
be accused by partisan opponents of
attempting to embarrass the adminis
tration. Now that the election is over
I shall exercise a privilege of discussing
the returns.
"Compared with the election of 1896
the Republicans have gained in some
places and lost in others. It was not a
sweeping Republican victory. On the
whole the result is not surprising when
it is remembered that the administra
tion is just concluding a successful
war. While a majority of the soldiers
are probably anti-Republican the man
agement of the war has been entirely in
Republican hands, and the strongest
argument used during the campaign
was that a Republican defeat would
discredit V. \ President in the eyes of
foreign nations.
"It was not a trail upon the issues
now before the people, but a successful
plea for a continuance of the case. The
people have not accepted the gold
standard; they have not fallen in love
with the plan to give the banks a mo
nopoly of the issue of paper money;
they have not decided to retire the
greenbacks; they have not surrendered
to the trusts.
"These questions were forced into the
background by the declaration of war,
but they must be faced again as soon
las peace is restored. The Chicago plat
! form presents for public consideration
I certain vital, economic questions'. That
; platform has not been abandoned by
j those who indorsed it in 1896. It will be
' reaffirmed in 1900, because it gives cx
i pression to the hopes and aspirations
of a large majority of the party.
"When the Democrats, Populists and
j Silver Republicans favored -Ã¼ban ln
' dependence they understood that war
would give a temporary advantage to
the party in power, but they were will-
Ing to risk defeat in order to aid the
people fighting to be free.
"Neither can the election be regarded
i as an indorsement of any definite foiÂ«
! eign policy. Until a treaty of peace has
! been entered into and the terms made
known the people cannot pass judg
ment upon it. Whether the war will
raise any question of sufficient import
ance to turn public attention away
from domestic problems remains to be
seen."
In regard to the Nebraska election he
said: "A liglv. vote was cast in Ne
braska, but the fusionists have elected
\ the entire State ticket and carried the
same Congressional districts that they
carried in 1896. If Senator Allen is de
' feated for re-elertion it will be because
Hf-nators are elected by legislators in-
I stead at by the people. If a Republican
i Senator is chosen by the next Legisla
ture he will go to Washington to repre
sent a minority of the people of the
State and to thwart the will of the
majority."
TWO SANTA CRTTZANS DEAD.
Alexander Russell and Louis M. Cox
Pass Away.
SANTA CRUZ, Nov. 15.â Alexander
Russell died suddenly at 10:30 o'clock this
morning at Watsonvllle from heart dis
ease. He left this city yesterday after
noon for Watsonville on business. He
was a member of the Society of Pioneers
and had resided since early days lift this
city. He was a man of means, owning
among other valuable property, the Rus
set] Tract, where all the State militia en
campments were held, and was also a
large stockholder in the City Bank. He
was a native of Ireland and 58 years old.
Louis M. Cox, one of the old settlers,
died this morning at the age of 70 years.
He waa a native of New York,
GLOOMY OUTLOOK
FOR MRS. BOTKIN
Many Witnesses Coming
From Delaware.
DUNNING TO BE AMONG THEM
SAYS HE WILL SHOW UP THE
FEMALE FIEND.
Attorney General White Will Per
sonally Assist at the Trial and
Is Confident of Con
viction.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
WILMINGTON, Del., Nov. 15.âAt
torney General Robert C. White to
night announced that he would go to
California to personally assist in the
trial of Mrs. Cordelia Botkin. The thir
teen witnesses from Delaware and the
Attorney General will leave in time to
be present at the opening of the trial.
Among the witnesses from Delaware
will be John P. Dunning, the husband
of one of the murdered women. Dun
ning talked freely of his trip to-day.
He said:
"It will be frightfully unpleasant, and
I shall be put in a bad light, I know,
but I'll show that female fiend up with
out any mercy. It is easier for a man
to have his past life thrown before the
public presence in a distant country. I
might have hesitated had it been here,
but there nothing shall be held back."
Dunning expressed a firmer opinion
than ever that Mrs. Botkin was the
slayer of his wife, although he consid
ered her trial in California a farce, and
that the only place for speedy justice
to be done was Delaware.
Asked what defense the California
woman intended to lean upon, Dunning
intimated that her strongest defense
was to further fight a trial. He had
no fear either for himself or for the
safety of the case as to what the wo
man intended to bring out.
"Of course," he added, "she will not
spare me, and I am very willing that
nothing should be spared."
Attorney General White explained
how the authorities intend to allow
Mrs. Botkin *. > loupholo for escape. No
technicalities â  f the law will save the
woman now. Should one jury disagree
she will be tried and retried. Should
she be acquitted the authorities will
then put her on trial for the murder of
Mrs. Deane, and having gotten a
glimpse of the defense she makes can
surely gain a conviction. This failing,
the appeal from the California Supreme
Court will be taken through the Federal
court and the accused woman will then
be tried for the double murder, which,
not being the same offense as either 01
the former causes, will not place her
life in jeopardy twice, as the constitu
tion prohibits.
Detective McVey, who is one of the
thirteen witnesses, will be sent on in
advance, to be ready at any moment
with his Federal court appeal or to
thwart any motive to prevent or delay
a trial or secure the release of the al
leged wholesale poisoner.
LIVELY RAILROAD RIVALRY.
Two Competing Lines Being Located
Up in Idaho.
PORTLAND. Or.. Nov. 15.â A special to
the Oregon ian from Lewiston, Idaho,
says:
The Northern Pacific and Oregon Rail
road and Navigation Company are locat
ing lines in the Nez Perce country, Idaho.
Appearances Indicate a lively contest be
tween the two companies, it looks as if
there will be plenty of railroad building
in the North Pacific Coast States this sea
son. The Northern Pacific is the aggres
sor. The Navigation Company is sup
posed to be backed by the Union Pacific
and the Great Northern, and the con
test promises to be a lively one. It looks
as if dividends will go into the new rail
lines instead of to the stockholders.
FOUND DEAD IN A SLOUGH.
Charles Lambrecht of Scandia Lost
on a Hunting Trip.
SITISUN, Cal., Nov. 15.â Charles I^am
brecht, son of Ludwig I,ambrfK"ht of
Scandia. near Suisun, who left his home
about 2 o'clock last Saturday afternoon
on a hunting trip in Mnntezuma Slough,
was found about dusk this evening in the
mud about fifty yards from his home.
When the unfortunate young man left
home ho promised to return early the
same evening. The parents became
alarmod when the hour became late and
their son failed to return. Neighboring
friends started in search, but were unable
to find any trace of him until late this
evening, when the body was found by
Claus Lambrecht about ten yards from
the wharf covered with mud.

LEARNED A LESSON.
The Poolrooms Paid Thompson Tick
ets and Are Now Regret
ting the Fact.
The latest returns which have turned
the tables in the Curry and Thompson
fight have taught the proprietors of the
large poolrooms of the city a lesson they
will not forget in a hurry. The first re

by the Western Union Telegraph Com
pany were incorrect, and that instead of
Thompson being elected he had been de
feated by a plurality of over 4000 votes.
These are the facts in the case as they
have been revealed up to date, but it was
the first few days after the election,
when the election of Thompson seemed a
certainty, that the poolrooms began to
pay off" on these two candidates. The
State ticket seemed a sure thing as early
as last Tuesday night week, and when
Wednesday passed without anything be
ing received to upset the first figures, the
men who receive wagers for a considera
tion began to think it was a question of
a dead cock in the pit, so whenever a
man showed up with a Thompson ticket
he got the cash without any questions
being asked. The prominent poolroom
men are ashamed to own that they, who
are supposed to be onto a thing or two,
have been taken in, and are keeping their
losses a secret.- As a matter of fact, not
tion, but still there was enough
placed in this city on Curry
and Thompson to make a snug
sum. It means a dead loss to the men
who do the cashing, but they can't af
ford to kick because of the laugh that
they would get.
On the other hand, in many instances
those that held tickets on Curry destroy
ed them after the first returns seemed
such a certainty, and now they are kick
ing themselves for their simplicity.
Quite a number, however, held on to the
bits of pasteboards as souvenirs of their
lack of judgment, and like the wise men,
are now saying: "I told you so."
There is no Way of getting at the
amount that was paid out by the pool
rooms, but it\can be safely stated that
nearly all tickets held on Thompson were
cashed. It is reported that some Repub
licans, even after they received the first
returns, refused to believe that Curry had
been defeated and backed up their con
victions with the cash, so that now they
are giving the knowing smile to their
less wise brethren.
DEATH OF PROFESSOR
THOMAS A. KENNEDY
CHICAGO, Nov. 15.â Professor Thomas
A. Kennedy of London. England, died at
the home of his sister in this city to-day
of Bright's disease. Professor Kennedy
is a well-known hypnotist and had just
arrived in Chicago from Melbourne. Aus
tralia, where he had just finished a two
years' engagement. He was taken sud
denly ill in Melbourne and left at once
for this country. He had intended to
make a tour of the United States before
returning to England. He is well. known
in San Francisco, where he had lived a
number of years.

SANTA CRUZ. Nov. 15.â Zach Xevers

The complaining witness is Justice Gard
ner, who lately ran for office and whom
Xevers attacked in a printed circular.

could congratulate themselves on the fact that he would remain in office for
two years longer. At that time the McDonalds had sent Sherman, Johns and
- others to the State Capitol to lobby through the Legislature a bill for the
division of Fresno County and the creation of Madera County.

Sherman was not only fearful of the
blackmail of local papers, but feared
those in the southern part of the State.
He informed Dick that a great paper
in Southern California was about to
change hands, and he would have It so
arranged that the McDonalds could use

erj-, official corruption and private dis

slightest disturbance to his conscience.
He saw immorality only in the efforts
of any one to secure money from him.
That seemed to have been the only
offense he understood and he bordered
on hysteria when he discovered that a
man to whom he had already given
$1000 demanded $1400 more. In Mr.
Sherman's code of ethics, as revealed
in his remarkable letters, It was a
stroke of shrewd business policy and
highly commendable to corrupt the At
torney General of this State, to buy
the silence of newspapers and to bribe
City Councils. It is probable that in
the history of great events on this
coast, in affairs that involved millions
of dollars and the reputations of prom
inent men, there is no parallel to these
letters. While Sherman discussed his
own corrupt methods he found time to
dream of fabulous wealth in new yen*
tures, and he suggested to Dick Mc-
Donald the absurd project of floating
five million of dollars of bonds on the
London market.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, NOVEMBER 16, 1898.
OF INTEREST TO PACIFIC AND PEOPLE'S HOME
SAVINGS DEPOSITORS.
When The Call began the first complete exposure and
disclosure of the crimes and offenses which wrecked the
Pacific Bank and robbed thirteen thousand depositors in
the People's Home Savings Bank it said expressly that it
was not for any purpose of newspaper sensation, but to
vindicate the general soundness of our financial institutions
which withstood the panic of 1893 and to point to the de
spoiled depositors a way to partial recovery of their
losses.
The Call is in possession of proof which makes it pos
sible for those depositors to better themselves. The Call
has evidence to prove that conveyance of property and as
sets belonging to the wrecked banks is vitiated by fraud
and it is at the disposal of- the robbed depositors if they
will put their interests in the hands of any legal represen
tative who can communicate with this paper.
This evidence will be published in its proper order,
and The Call makes this announcement in order that it may
inure to the interests of the depositors and not to the ad=
vantage of speculators in their misfortunes.

looked half sick the day I was there.
Very truly, M. H. SHERMAN.

Spence was that he hoped Spence
would tell Mr. Maybury and he would
make it unpleasant for R. H. Jr.
He has a piece of paper on which
he has a list of all the assets of the
Pacific Bank and he goes around and
shows this to prove that the bank is
not solvent and cannot pay off its de
positors, and he took this list with
him to S. F. and showed it around
there and has done all he could to get
newspaper reporters investigating
.this subject.
This I learned on good authority. I
know that R. H. Jr. and D. S. have
got the Chronicle and the Examiner
all O. X., and that we will have no
trouble with them and will succeed in
accomplishing much, still, all these
things are very disagreeable and very
humiliating and they make me feel
more anxious every day to try and
help turn everything that I am in any
way interested* in into cash to put the
same in your bank.
Please do not worry, because worry
and anxiety is what kills people more
than hard work. Let's have only
kind words ami kind thoughts for each
other and thoughts of sympathy,
for the Lord knows we are all having
a hard enough time. Very truly,
M. H. SHERMAN.

Ing blood up and the second eleven fought

territory. As a result of the fierce plung

tain Fisher, who was laid out in a scrim

mans. Downing, Fickert and Harrelsonâ

guard, was shifted to the second this
evening and Cairns and Eaton, the fresh
men guards, were tried on the college
eleven. Gilman and Blglow are fighting
hard for the center position. Biglow

noon. Anderson is being trained as cen-

Long runs by Plate and Daggett. the
college halves, and hard line bucking
were the order of the day. Murphy was
unlucky in his tries at field goals, the
line not holding firmly and the passes
from center being poor.

Thursday, aijd the team will leave for
training at Congress Springs on Friday.

NORTHWESTERN PROJECTS.
Proposed New Sea and Rail Line to
Dawson.
W. L. Bernard, editor of the Alberta
Tribune, now stopping at the Brooklyn
Hotel, gives some interesting- Informa
tion concerning the proposed railroad be
tween Calgary, N. W. T., and Dawson
City.
"Calgary," he said last evening, "is in
a fair way to become the capital of a
larger Alberta, which will Include the val
uable Peace River country, through
which the line of railroad connecting
Calgary and Edmonston with Dawson
City is expected to run. A belief pre
vails that such a railroad would be a
profitable investment, as it would run
Through a fertile country, suitable for all
kinds of ranching and farming.
"Workshops are now being erected at
Calgary by the Canadian Pacific at an
expense of JIOO.OOO, with an estimated pay
roll of about $10,000 per month. These
â hops are expected to be In operation
early next year, and, in consequence,
the rentals of business property in Cal
gary have advanced 100 per cent and much
building is going on.
"What most interests San Francisco
is the line of steamers between Van
couver, San Francisco and Southern Cali
fornia ports (projected by the Vancouver
and Dawson City Shiping Agency) which
has already received the approval of the
Vancouver City Council and Board of
Trade.
"In the extension of trade, too, it should
be of material benefitâ giving to the resi
dents of Alberta the products of Califor
nia and to California the cheap prices of
Alberta's wonderful dairy products. It is
my opinion, too, that such a line would
not necessarily interfere with the exist
ing traffic between Victoria and San
Franciscoâ it would create a distinct trade
of its own."
MECHANICS' INSTITUTE.
The New Librarian Confers With the
Members of the Board â A
Cafe Suggested.
At the semi-monthly meeting of the
Mechanics' Library trustees last evening
Librarian Taggart was introduced to the
members of the board by President Den
ioke. The new librarian made a brief
reply to the. words of welcome by the
president, after which routine business
was disposed of.
The board, at the request of Miss VV al
laee, voted to donate 100 volumes to Bat
tery E. Third Artillery, stationed at Fort
Potot, to form the nucleus of a library tor
the enlisted men.
William Mitchell sent a communication
suggesting that the board of trustees es
tablish a cafe in connection with the li
brary. He also proposed that the insti
tute'arrange to give a series of popular
concerts during the winter.
The police drum corps asked permission
to use the Pavilion to practice in on Tues
day and Friday evenings.
The matter of basing the Institute
grounds at Sixteenth and Folsom streets
was referred to a special committee. The
present tenant's lease expires January 1.
and he wishes to renew at $50 a month.
An offer of $100 a month has been received
from another party and the committee
will investigate the matter and report at
the next meeting.
THE FORT BAKER SCANDAL.
Lieutenant Bennett Hopes to Have
His Report Ready Soon.
Lieutenant Bennett was busy yesterday
preparing his report of the Fort Baker
investigation with a view of submitting it
to the adjutant general. The lieutenant
will go to Fort Baker to-day for the pur
pose of making inquiries Into the conduct
of the canteen at that place. He expects
to have his report ready some time this
week.
Colonel Long, the depot quartermaster,
is confronted with a serious problem.. He
is of the opinion that the St. Paul will
not begin to carry the Christmas presents
that are being sent to the boys at Ma
nila by their relatives and friends. The
depot Is now filled to overflowing with the
boxes that have been sent in, and there
appears to be no end to them. Yesterday
Colonel Long received a letter from an
old lady living in the country, who has
a son at Manila. She stated that her
apple crop was not quite ripe and wanted
to know if the colonel could delay the
sailing of the vessel until such a time
as the apples might be ripe, as she de
sired to send some to her boy.
General Merriam stated yesterday that
it was not the intention of the Govern
ment to send any more troops here, but
that the troops already at the front, to
be ordered home, would be relieved by
those that recently sailed under General
Miller.

terday on the body of Mrs. Louisa Spain,

Minna streets. The Jury found a verdict

In the case of J. F. McDonald, steward

the vessel while on the way to this port,
and who died in this city from his in
juries, thp jury returned a verdict to the
effect that the accident occurred through
the carelessness of the deceased. There
was a rope around the hatchway and the
deceased was warned against the dan
ger, but he went Inside the rope and
slipped into the hold.
CATHOLICS GIVE VOICE
TO PRAYERS FOR RAIN
By order of Archbishop Rlordan masses were solemnized in all the
Catholic churches in the archdiocese Monday morning for the purpose of
invoking the blessing of rain. Although the season has long since com
menced when the dry weather should have been superseded by different
atmospheric conditions, there is absolutely not the slightest indication
that the needed change is likely to occur.
The farming interests of the Stale have already been threatened by the
drought and, as a consequence of this fact, and at the general request
of people, both interested and disinterested, all over the State, mass by
the authorization of the representative head of the Catholic church In
this archdiocese was celebrated and prayers offered up to God supplicat
ing the visitation of rain upon those who are now aÂ»most without hope
of saving their interests from ruin.
Praying for rain in this city is not. unusual. It will be remembered that
less than a year ago mass was solemnized in St. Mary's Cathedral, and,
in fact, in all the Catholic churches throughout the city and State, for
the same reason that they were celebrated last Monday morning. Special
prayer was inserted for the occasion and read in Latin from the altar
by the officiating priest. People crowded the edifice and joined in the.
general supplication to the "Ruler of the Elements" to visit upon the State
the blessing of rain as a salvation of the homes of farmers and the sal
tation of almost life itself, which the ruin of the crops and other agri
cultural interests throughout the State practically meant.
Whether as a result of pious supplication, or the consequence of the
natural workings of nature, rain came and continued to come for many
days after the celebration of mass throughout the State. The general
impression in the church, however, was that the welcome visitation came
as a result of prayer and not the spontaneous change of atmospheric pres
sure.
It was in consideration of this almost marvelous advent of rain in the
early part of the year, after months of almost continual drought, that
renewed prayers for the same blessing were offered up In the churches
Monday morning.
THE SAX FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, XOYEMBER 10, 1898.
SPIDER KELLY
AND MULLER
FIGHT A DRAW
Go the Full Route,
Twenty Rounds.
REILLY DEFEATS PETE M'GEE
MAYNARD AND McFADDEN BOX
THEIR FIFTH NO CONTEST.
Western tJlub Opens the Winter Sea
son of Sport With an Average
Lot of Bouts t*Â»
the Ring.
Spider Kelly and Fred Muller went the
full route, twenty rounds, in their boxing
bout at Woodward's Garden last night.
At the conclusion Referee Phil Wand de
cided it a draw, much to the displeasure
of the followers of Kelly. Kelly scored
many more points than his adversary,
but Muller was always on the aggressive.
When they stood off and boxed Kelly
always showed to best advantage. His
long- reach kept Muller where he could
do no damage. His favorite blow was a
peculiar back hand cross or chop which
he Invariably followed with a right on
the body. This took much of the speed
out of Muller, who was not over strong
when he entered the ring, owing to the
difficulty he encountered making the re
quired weight.
Several times during the bout each man
had his opponent in difficulty, and the
end seemed imminent when the tired box
er would recover and hold his own until
the bell gave him a much needed respite.
Muller was invariably on tho aggressive,
but the majority of his well-meant blows
were stopped by the clever "Spider" be
fore they had worked any harm. Kelly
did not seem at ease until the ninth
round, when he commenced to jab Muller
and play for his heart. This had the
desired effect, as it stopped his gallop in
a marked degree for a time.
In the eleventh both men fought them
selves to a standstill. In the following
round Kelly had Muller dazed, catching
him twice with right crosses. Muller
stood the severest punishment and was
saved by the bell. Both men tried for a
knockout blow in the nineteenth round,
and the partisans of each man had many
opportunities to yell for their favorite.
The mm stood In the middle of the ring
and gave blow for blow, paying but little
heed to their guard.
The twentieth round was a hurricane,
each man in turn seeming on the verge
of going out. Kelly clinched repeatedly
to avoid punishment. Muller tried again
and again to land his right, but the agile
Kelly seemed never to be at the place
where the blow was directed. When the
gong sounded the end of the round and
of the fight both were hammering away
In workmanlike manner and paid no heed
to the signal. The referee and the sec
onds had finally to separate them and
drag them 10 their corners.
Referee Wand held up both hands to
signify a draw. The adherents of Kelly
seemed In the majority, as their hissoO
completely drowned out the applause of
th"sÂ«- who thought the decision a just
one. The Kelly men claimed that on a
tally of clean blows landed their repre
sentative won easily. On the other hand
the Muller men held that he did all the
leading and was the aggressor through
out. He mixed things frequently and
then showed to good advantage.
Kelly was splendidly handled by Young
Mitchell, Tim McGrath and Frank Mc-
Connell. Muller was looked after by Mc-
Devitt. Hines and Gus Muller. Muller
seemed too finely drawn, while the
"Spider" was at his natural weight.
There was a great deal of sameness
about the rounds, each man having his
favorite blows and using them through
out. There was plenty of action, but not
much damage done. Kelly wore Muller
down with his punches over the heart,
and which Muller seemed unable to get
away from, especially when they followed
the "chop" blow.
Kid Maynard and Kid McFadden fought
their fifth draw. They have met six
times, Maynard getting the decision on
one occasion. They went at it hammer
and tongs last nigjit and neither could
obtain a decisive lead. Maynard. who is
a hard hitter, had the best of it up to the
ninth round. McFadden then shook his
old gold locks of hair and by his aggres
siveness made it a stand-off with his
game bantam opponent. Maynard land
ed the most clean blows, but could not
stop his sturdy opponent. The lads are
gladiators in miniature and their bout
pleased the spectators. Each apparently
thought he had earned the decision, but
the announcement of a draw by Referee
Wand was loudly applauded.
The opening bout of iÂ«e evening proved
an appetizer for the hard fighting that
followed. Jimmy Rielly of local fame and
Pete McGee, an unknown quantity, were
pitted against each other for ten rounds.
Rielly was possessed of more science
than his opponent, but what McGee lack
ed in that direction he made up in grit,
a long reach and strength. In the first
four rounds Rielly had McGee profuseiy
bleeding from tue nose, but the latter
did not mind it in the least. He met
Rielly's vicious attacks with straight
lefts and hard rights until the tide of bat
tle turned in his favor. Despite the en
couragement given McGee by a clamorous
gallery and a howling pit. Rielly kept his
man busy ducking hooks and jabs. In
ducking McGee proved himself clever and
saved himself much punishment. In the
eighth and ninth rounds the gore that
flowed from McGee's proboscis covered
both men. makin^ them take on a sorry
appearance. Rielly started to end hos
tilities in the ninth, but was met with a
determined resistance. During the last
minute of the round he landed a heavy j
right on the jaw and quickly followed it
up with additional blows, making McGee
grogtry. In the cheering that ensued dur
ing the rally neither the referee, seconds
nor fighters' heard the mournful clang of
the gong, and the men fought on.
McGee was knocked down at this junc
ture, and Captain Gillen seeing his gory
appearance stopped hostilities. Referee
ALLEGED EXTORTIONISTS ON TRIAL
Breezy Tales of tie Efforts of Elsie Williams and Her Friends
to Secure Captain Nevill's Coin.
WITNESS REMINGTON. KLSIE WILLIAMS. MYKON AZHDERIAN.
JUDGE COOK, a jury and a
court room full of spectators
listened yesterday to the ef
forts of the prosecution to il
lustrate the alleged manner
from Captain William A. Nevills.
in which Elsie Williams, Grace
Loose and Myron Azhderian are
alleged to have extorted money
The State's interests were in the
hands of Thomas Riordan, Reel B.
Terry and Frank H. Short of
Fresno; and the defense was con
ducted by George W. Baker and L.
Clark of Fresno.
The defendants are accused of
having in a particular instance ex
tended the nether extremity of the
aged capitalist, to the tune of $2000,
by threats of exposure of his re
lations to the woman Williams.
This act is said to have been per
formed by the latter and her con
federates while Captain Nevills
was lying sick at the Palace Hotel
and unable to stand off his perse
cutors.
Elsie is petite, painted, and in
earlier days must have been rather
pretty, with doll features and pecu
liar gray eyes. Her figure is dain
ty and her appearance In silks and
sealskins yesterday accounts for
the tact that she fascinated the
wealthy captain greatly to his
sorrow. The other woman is com
monplace and Azhderian is a
swart, hatchet-faced, black-eyed,
shrewd looking young man.
The prosecution, after estab
lishing the fact that Captain Nev
ills had given the defendant Elsie a
check for $2000, supposedly for the
purpose of keepin~ her mouth
closed as to his intimacy with her,
called a number of witnesses resi
dent in Fresno and this city to
show by inference and otherwise
that the character and reputation
of Elsie Williams was not enviable.
The young woman appeared to en-
Joy this part of the proceedings
thoroughly, and when it was tes
tified that Azhderian had shared a
cottage with her over night; that
but one sleeping apartment had
been occupied, and that a man's
shoes were seen in her room,
while Azhderian was out in the
yard in his bare feet, she enjoyed
a hearty laugh and succeeded in
blushing through her paint.
William Remington, who testified
to these facts, related several con
versations he had had with Azh
dertan calculated to show that the
latter was working to "pull the
leg" of Nevills. The fact was
also brought out that Nevills had
strictly forbidden Azhderian, his
superintendent, to stay at night
on the ranch where Elsie Williams
and Grace Loose were quartered.
However, the young man paid no
attention to these orders and spent
his time with the women when the
"boss" was away.
On one occasion Nevills came
Joe Acton awarded the decision to Rielly
am Id the howls of disapproval from the
gallery. Had McGee been properjy han
dled he would have gone another round
anrl gained a draw, as he was doing ex
cellent work.
The attendance was an average one. be
ing made up chiefly of the men always
seen at the ringside. Managers Man in
Murphy and C. H. Long had all their ar
rangements in splendid order, the only
drawback being the dim. religious light in
which the men boxed. The first brace of
boxers entered the ring at 8:30. and there
was no delay between any of the bouts.

by them in every way a success, such of

home unexpectedly, and the re
sult was that Azhderian fled to his

than face the wrath of the cap
tain. The relation rtf this painful
incident also filled Elsie with mer
riment, and caused her to long and
carefully stare at the tips of her
boots.
Remington, in the course of his
testimony, stated that he had sev
eral talks with Azhderian, which
convinced witness that the young
man and the women were planning
to despoil Nevills of many thou
sands of his good iron dollars, but
what his scheme was he did not
know. On one occasion, while he
was painting a cottage that Azh
derian had built for himself Wit
ness asked if Azhderian intended
to marry Elsie. The young man
laughed and replied in the nega
tive.
"He explained." said Remington,
"that the girls were 'dead fly'; that
there would be a crash at the
ranch before long, and he was pre
paring for it. It was his intention,"
continued witness, "to entice me
big fruit-growers to his cottage, get
them under the inlluence of the
women and champagne, and then
make valuable contracts for fruit.
If they kicked when they became
sobered threats of exposure would
silence them. It was Azhderian's
intention after working this part of
the game to employ the same
methods with the fruit-buyers in
order to sell his contracts for high
P "^ was further brought out in the
testimony of this witness that
A*h4erian had visited him and
promised to substantially reward
him if he did not appear against
Af.biVrian in a damage suit the
latter has instituted against
Nevills.
One of the witnesses stated that
he had seen Azhrierian sitting on
the lap of Elsie Williams.
"Well, if It were a c: ime to sit
In n girl's lap. Mr. Riordan." .said
Attorney Baker, "you would have
been In "jail long ago."
"I plead guilty for myself arid
you, too," replied Riordan prompt
ly, and the courtroom smiled a ldi
bly.
Considerable cumulative evidenca
was introduced tending to show ef
forts on the part of the defend
ants to extort wealth from Nevills
The witnesses were Mary VV New
comb. J. D. Dunn. Cashier HcPer
of Wells-Fargo's Bank. E. D. Leo,
Frederick Encson, W. B. Lyon,
David Stutzman. John Ranlolph.
Nevills' colored valet, and William
Remington.
The jury is composed of the fol
lowing: A. Hirsch. D. W. Lohsen,
J. J. Hayer, W. E. Lovejoy. W.
Griffen, H. Bendit. R. Mehrtens,

Hawaiian curios generously loaned them
for exhibition. The result was that the
spacious rooms were filled with an aggre
gation of articles which were rich and
beautiful 'enough to make any profes
sional collector's heart burn with envy.
Pictures of all descriptionsâ paintings,
photographs and sketches, all illustrative
of Hawaiian life, personality or scenery
covered the walls, hung against a back
ground of blossom and verdure. Tapa
cloth and mats of finest weave, artistical
ly draped alcoves and corners; baskets,
musical instruments, specimens of royal
China and silver ware, carvings, bead
dresses, fans and hundreds of other arti
cles were grouped on tables and on tem
porary shelves: Hawaiian and American
flags "were intertwined over the windows
and doorways, and palms and ferns and
trailing vines made a mimic forest of the
whole interior.
The rooms were crowded both after
noon and evening, and a very satisfactory
sum was realized for the very worthy ob
ject of the entertainment. During the
afternoon Mrs. A. D. Sharon read a very
interesting original paper on "Hawaii,
Past and Present." Charles Mayer played
several beautiful Hawaiian melodies upon
the zither, including the always lovely
"Aloha Oe." Harry Monges sang a tenor
solo Miss Levison acting as accompanist:
Mrs. W. H. Mills sang the "Star Spangled
Banner." and Miss Kirby. Miss Constance
and Miss Kathleen de Young,' Miss Mar
gory Mills and Miss Edith Leale. under
the'direction of Miss Edith Howland. sang
a number of Hawaiian airs, accompany
ing themselves on four ukuleles and two
taro-patch fiddles.
The evening programme included songs
and dances by some native Hawaiian mon
in costume: music on lute and mandolin
by Professor Samuel Adelstein: an ad
dress by Miss Craft; vocal music by Miss
Xenia Roberts and Miss Millie Flynn;
piano solos by H. J. Stewart and Martin
Schultz. and an address by S. M. Short
ridge.
