THE CITY AND THE CITIZEN.
\A ORE than ordinary interest attaches to the
/ \ current number of the Merchants' Association
* * Review by reason of the publication in full of
the address of President F. W. Dohrmann giving a
report of his observations of municipal government
during his recent tour throughout the Eastern States
and Europe. The address is an able one, and de
serves the careful study of every citizen who takes an
intelligent interest in San Francisco and has enough
civic patriotism to desire to improve its conditions.
That Mr. Dohrmann found in the great cities he
visited much that should be imitated or emulated in
San Francisco goes without saying. A municipality
so young as ours has always a great deal which it can
Itarn with profit from older communities. The main
interest of the address, however, does not lie in what
is said <^f our shortcomings as compared with older
cities, but in the plans suggested for remedying them.
It has long been known that we need better sewers,
better paved and cleaner streets, a public library
building commensurate to the needs of a city of this
size, and a more efficient administration of civic af
fairs generally. The question of vital interest is: How
shall we attain these things?' To that question Mr.
Dohrmann addresses himself with earnestness and
with hopefulness.
Two plans are suggested. The first proposes that
the Merchants' Association perfect a programme of
municipal work and then call a large and thoroughly
representative convention of the people to consider
k and revise it. To carry out this programme a non
partisan organization is to be effected and a ticket of
candidates put into the field pledged to undertake it
if elected. Under this plan political parties would be
set aside and a strictly municipal party would appeal
tc the people for support.
The second plan proposes that the programme ap
proved by the convention be submitted to all candi
dates of existing parties, and the votes of the support
ers of the programme given to those who would be
most likely to uphold it in office. If any party refuse
to approve the programme in its platform, then the
candidates of that party are to be opposed with might
and main.
That strong objections can be urged to either or
both of these plans Mr. Dohrmann is well aware, and
he frankly says so. He is sanguine, however, that if
one of them be adopted and firmly supported^>y a
considerable body of resolute, earnest men, a great
work can be accomplished for the improvement of the
city. Nor can there be any serious doubt that he is
right. San Francisco will have a better local govern
ment whenever the progressive and influential men
of the city co-operate to effect it.
â¢It is not advisable to start a non-partisan movement
to that end. for in the United States all such move
ments are futile. What has been accomplished in
Boston and Philadelphia affords evidences of what
can be achieved here by party government if only the
right sort of public sentiment be formed to support
good government.
The first thing to be done is to impress upon the
citizen the sense of his personal responsibility to the
community. Upon that point Mr. Dohrmann was
both clear and strong in his utterances. "Let us," he
said, "unite and pledge ourselves and our best efforts.
Let us do our duty to ourselves, our homes and our
posterity. Let us at this time lay the foundation
broad and deep, so that, future generations may ap
preciate our efforts and benefit by them. Let us see
tc it that our city will fulfill its manifest destiny, which
is, to be not only one of the greatest but one of the
best of all dwelling-places on earth."
It is difficult to understand the system of logic em
ployed by the so-called man who, because angry with
an individual, stabbed with a pitchfork a worthy and
inoffensiv^ horse, the property of this individual. By
the way, the County Jail is marked by a pleasing ab-
sence of both horses and pitchforks, and society owes
tp certain citizens that they be safeguarded against
temptation.
Soldiers who without warrant search private houses
may sooner or later run against a citizen who will
voice his objections through the convincing method
of using a shotgun on them. The fact that his house
is his castle will be regarded as sufficient warrant.
Even if the United States take the Philippines
from Spain there will be the insurgents still to deal
with. They will never give a quitclaim deed without
considerable preliminary haggling.
There is no occasion for the Filipinos to appeal to
America. This country has plenty of representatives
at the islands who will give respectful hearing to a
recital of native woes.
If the pound is to remain in politics it should be
used for the impounding of politicians.
THE SAN FBAN CISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, NOVEMBER 16, 1898.
THE FALSEHOOD ABOUT VflN FLEET.
JV yi R. JUSTICE VAN FLEET was defeated for
/ \ election to the Supreme bench by a rank
*â  * falsehood started by the Examiner and propa
gated in the fusion press.
This falsehood was to the effect that Justice Van
Fleet, in the Fox case, had decided that a poor man
was not entitled to as much damages for the killing of
his child by a railroad as a rich man. A large number
of Superior Court Judges all over the State, Demo
crats and Republicans, among them Judge Budd, the
father of Governor Budd, appeared in denial of the
Examiner falsehood and slander. They certified that
Justice Van Fleet had made no such decision, but that
the judgment of the Supreme Court in the Fox case,
in which the whole bench concurred, was simply a
correct statement of the law of compensatory dam
ages in such cases, and that the law is so well settled
that the same decision would be given in any Supreme
Court in the United States.
It is plain, then, that Justice Van Fleet was beaten
by a most arrant slander and outrageous falsehood.
It goes without saying that if the bench is to be con
trolled by such means, and men are to be beaten or
elected by the circulation of lies, our whole judicial
system is apt to be destroyed, and the administration
of justice to be put at the mercy of any wretch who
commands the publicity necessary to the hurtful cir
culation of a lie. The offense of the Examiner, while
making Justice Van Fleet its immediate victim, is in
its essence a crime against the personal and property
rights of every citizen, since it subjects the courts
which protect those rights to the control of liars and
slanderers.
The Examiner has. since the election, sought to
justify itself and to maintain its position by con
tinuing to repeat its falsehood.
In a recent issue it says: "Now. as a matter of fact,
the rule of loss of service as a measure of damages
is obsolete. It is never followed because it is impos
sible and absurd. * * * If there is any reason why
the grief of a father or mother over the death of a
child, caused by gross negligence, should not be con
sidered in damages, we fail to see it."
Now, on the contrary, the rule of loss of service is
the only rule followed in every American court, be
cause it is the only feasible rule. The Supreme Court
of California followed that rule in the Fox case be
cause it is the only rule by which a measure of dam
ages can be found. Instead of being a rule that works
against the poor man damaged by the death of his
child, it is his special protection, for the reason that
irs the homes of the poor children must earlier begin
service that brings bread to the table. This service
may be only in the daily duties of the household, at
first of the simplest nature, but duties which, if dis
charged by the parent, abridge his wage-earning
labors and entail loss upon him. In the homes of the
rich these services are not exacted of the child, nor
i- it required to be a wage-earner outside to con
tribute to the family support. Therefore it is plain
that the rule which the Examiner falsely says is ob
solete is a rule followed by all our courts for the ben
efit of the poor- man instead of to his detriment. Had
Justice Van Fleet treated it as obsolete he would
really have taken from the poor man a protection
whjch he enjoys in the courts.
The lying perversion of the Examiner has led to
the injury of Justice Van Fleet at the hands of the
very kind of people in whose favor he upheld a rule
of law which is for their benefit and protection.
An inspection of the dictum of the Examiner that
parental grief is the proper rule of damages demon
strates its folly. If the household service of a child
save to its parent one hour a day, it saves to him an
hour's wages. If his earnings are, say, 25 cents an
hour, there is a concrete basis on which to estimate
damages. The natural conclusion that the child, when
qualified by age to enter upon a wage-earning occu
pation, before reaching its majority, will have an
earning capacity equal to that of its father's vocation,
carries this concrete basis of damages through to the
end of the child's minority. On this basis damages
are estimable. They may be mathematically
measured.
But passing to the rule upon which the Examiner
insists, a rule unknown to the courts and foreign to
the law, there is no basis at all for the estimate and
measure of damages. Who can express in dollars
and cents a mental emotion? Who can measure the
varying degrees of grief and assess a money value to
them? What sum of money can pay for grief and
compensate the sufferer for sorrow?
The law is an exact science. It deals with concrete
things, with the material, and not the immaterial and
ineffable. Therefore it cannot measure grief, nor put
a value on ineffable emotions. The effect of grief, and
its prolongation, depend upon individual tempera
ment. In many instances sorrow for the dead leads
to suicide. What court 01 jury can make money com
pensation in damages for such a grief?
It is plain, therefore, that the only rule of damages
is the one established by the law, followed by all the
courts and stated in the decision written by Justice
Van Fleet and declared to be the law by Judge Budd
and jurists of the Superior Courts of all parties.
The Examiner beat Justice Van Fleet by lying.
Its act was no more honorable than if it had sent a
man to his house with a forged message to his wife
for his watch and purse for the purpose of stealing
his property. Its defense of its lying since the elec
tion is as dishonorable as the original offense. Every
Judge, Justice, lawyer and thinking man in the State
knows that it used falsehood to affect an election to
the Supreme bench, and that by so doing it struck an
envenomed blow at the courts, thereby weakening
and seeking to destroy the judicial safeguards set
around person and property in this State.
To his imperishable honor be it said that Senator
White denounced from the fusion stump this Exam
iner lie about Justice Van Fleet. So should all good
men denounce and resent it, and punish the paper's
attempt to justify it since its deception of the people
worked Justice Van Fleet's rejection at the polls.
THE REVOLT AGAINST BfIILEY.
WHEN Bailey of Texas was elevated to the posi
tion of leader of the Democratic party in the
House at the beginning of the present Con
gress there was joy in the party, for it was believed
the leadership would have in it something of the
vigor, the energy and the animation of youth. Dem
ocracy had grown weary of her futile old war-horses
and received the young man with the ardor of great
hopes.
It is known to all the nation how disappointing to
the House, to his party and to the country the new
leader has been. The contests between the two par
ties during the last session were not many nor were
they of great moment, but they were sufficient to
show that Bailey has neither tact nor force in dealing
with men; neither statesmanship nor political
sagacity in dealing with great issues. He did not
distinguish himself in any way except by refusing to
attend a dinner where he would be expected to wear a
dress coat.'
As a natural result of these deficiencies there is *
widespread revolt against him in all sections of his
party. Not only are nearly all the Democratic Con
gressmen from the Northern States to oppose him
in the next Congress, but many of his Southern col
leagues will unite with them.
The argument of the revolters is one of half con
temptuous opposition. For example, the Courier-
Journal, speaking for Kentucky Democrats, recently
said the party would have been much better off if the
Democrats of the House at the last session of Con
gress "had stuck a stick of candy in Babe Bailey's
mouth and thus kept him occupied."
When that view is taken of his authority it is clear
Mr. Bailey's career as a Democratic leader is about
over. There is no telling who will be his successor,
but the young man from Texas will take a back seat
in the next Congress.
THE DEfID NON-PARTISANS.
THE criticism of a correspondent that in sum
marily disposing of the late unlamented Non-
Partisan party we have been unnecessarily se
vere calls for a brief explanation. Our correspondent
will note that The Call has never charged the Non-
Partisans with corruption. On the contrary, we have
always given them the credit of a pure intention and
exalted motive, reserving for a few of their leaders
only the suspicion of knavery and cunning. It is not
exactly complimenting their intelligence to say so,
but we have always considered the Non-Partisans a
band of well-meaning old fellows, the chief end of
whose political existence was to have their legs pulled.
This leg-stretching never became painfully con
spicuous, however, until Mayor Phelan and his
brother-in-law took charge of Deacon Fitch's or
ganization. These gentlemen succeeded in completely
exposing the game which has been so cunningly
played by the other manipulators of Non-Partisan
politics, and the fact that Mr. Fitch's indorsement be
came worthless in the late contest was largely due to
the rawness of their methods.
While we may not entertain a great deal of respect
for the penetration of the estimable old gentlemen
who comprise the bulk of the Non-Partisan "conven
tion," we do not hesitate to disclaim any suspicion of
their motives or integrity. Perhaps they are not to be
censured for falling victims to the wiles of the job
chasing Democratic politicians; better men than they
have succumbed to conspiracies designed to deprive
representative citizens of their political freedom. But
we do blame Mr. Fitch for being taken in. He is an ex
perienced boss and manipulator, a thorough politician
and an excellent judge of human nature. There is
no excuse for him. The fat and unctuous Non-Par
tisans might have been fooled, but the lean and
hungry Fitch, like Cassius, was not foolable.
However, non-partisanism in San Francisco, hav
ing been thoroughly exposed, is now a closed inci
dent. In writing its epitaph we have endeavored to
be as kind as the truth permits, and our correspondent
entirely misapprehends the purport of our previous
remarks if he thinks we have intended to reflect at all
upon the character of the old fossils who mainly com
posed Deacon Fitch's late "convention."
The Non-Partisans meant well, but their theory of
politics was wrong and they were "worked" by job
chasing Democratic politicians. There is no such
thing as non-partisanism in politics. It is a delusion
and a snare. It is always a resource of the bosses for
purloining votes. Politics is the science of- getting
the offices, and that is partisan to the last degree.
Only dead politicians are non-partisans. All others
are violently partisan.
BURGLARY AND THE HONEYMOON.
ANOTHER burglar has made the plea that he
stole so as to provide food for his wife, a
bride of six weeks. This plea is battered,
frayed, played out. If true in this particular case, it
only serves to aggravate the offense. Nobody has
any business to get married unless sure of flour and
potatoes enough to last at least through a reasonable
honeymoon. At the expiration of this period the
bride could better take in washing or kick the groom
out and go back to the old folks than permit herself
to be pampered on the fruits of nocturnal raids.
There are comparatively few able-bodied people
who are obliged to go hungry, although they may be
short on pie. Circumstances have arisen under which
larceny is regarded as a moderate offense. A hungry
man snatching a loaf of bread is never by the law
viewed so austerely as the well-fed thief. Indeed, pub
lic sympathy is apt to be with him. He stands in a
more favorable light even than the kleptomaniac who
to the crime of stealing adds that of lying, and spreads
the cloak of a bogus respectability over the pair.
The particular burglar under consideration would
have had a better chance with a jury if he had pre
tended to be a subject of kleptomania, lifting portable
property simply because he could not help doing so,
and greatly to his own regret. Then he could have shed
a few tears, his lawyer could have spoken eloquently
of the lonely bride sitting in tears, and the prisoner
have gone his way. pitied as the victim of a relentless
malady. But deliberate burglary on, a pretense of
famine is without the pale of solicitude. We fear the
honeymoon has not merely been impaired, but re
sumption of it indefinitely postponed.
FUNNY PORIS PAPERS.
THE press of Paris is an instance of habitual
hysterics. Here it is railing at the "hypocrisy"
of the United States, while the whole world is
ringing with the most shocking revelations concern
ing France ever made of a civilized nation. The
Dreyfus affair, however it may terminate, has given
to the history of the French people a black and in
delible page. There has been shown to exist in offi
cial circles a depth and foulness of corruption such as
even a Zola could not adequately portray. Compared
with a truthful description of it the seething impurity
of the pool of Lourdes, set forth in fullest detail,
would afford pleasant reading.
The present is an excellent time for the papers of
France to first see their own house put in order. Let
them devote their energies to the revivification of
an honor which seems dead, to the purging of the
high places, the exposure and punishment of crime.
Let them correct the practices which cause a state
prisoner to commit suicide rather than lay bare his
secrets. Let them rebuke the army for acting as men
drunken on absinthe, and call upon the Government
to release the people from a despotism which, in
claiming to be a republic, stamps itself as a sham.
There is plenty for the French press to do. It has
troubles within the borders of its own country. The
impertinent and childish meddling with affairs which
in no manner concern it will after a while become ir
ritating, and perhaps cause remark.
The only consoling thought in connection with the
latest tragedy} on the yacht Chispa arises from an im
pression that the trio mixed up in the mess could all
have been spared.
It is only fair to say that some of the members of
the School Board are not suspected of honesty, and
they won't be while they continue to be so scared at
the prospect of an investigation.
Observer Hammon must stop predicting clear
weather if he wants to retain his popularity.
AN IDYL OF THE FERRY
Last night upon the upper deck there came a maiden there And while
the light was dim and low, so eyes could reach a foot or so. I think that she
was fair. She wore a sacque of sealskin long, the collar to her ears; the
â way her silken skirts frou-froued would drive a man to beers. Her nat,
it was a picture hat. in width about a yard, and though I could not see her
face, I hold it in regard. ....
She sat her down and braved the breeze, then blowing swift and chill,
it tore my whiskers, made me sneeze; I really thought that I would freeze,
and surely she'd get ill. But no, she took her picture hat and placed it in
her lap, defying winds to make her bangs encounter a mishap. (Please let
your accents fall just right or you will spoil this rhyme. I'd like to build
the thing anew, but really there's no time.) I asked her if she didn t fear
that she would catch a cold. It may be that she didn't hear, but still, low
down, I muchly fear, she thought me badly bold.
When soon Goat Island loomed up close she darted for the rail, I felt my
cheeks with terror quake the instant that she made the break, and eke
my knees grow pale. But quick I hastened in her wake prepared to make
a grab. She turned and daggers looked at me, for while too dark to clear
ly see, I felt the glances stab. The truth was that the frisky wind had
whisked her hat away, and I thought she meant suicide, and she thought I
got gay.
'Twas then I hurried down below and sought a wicked bar where
maids in sealskin never go, but consolations ebb and flow, and creature
comforts are. THE INNOCENT MASHER.

One of the
prominent mem
bers of the Ala
nieda delegation,
at the last Re
publican Conven
tion, held at Sac
ramento, was W.
Nil Friend of
lengthy persuasion. Of course, he and his
colleagues were extremely desirous of hav
ing Dr Pardee receive the gubernatorial
nomination, but their efforts in his be
half were futile and Gage plucked the
coveted plum. Naturally Friend felt
very disconsolate over the defeat of the
man on whom he had pinned his faith, but
when several other candidates for nomi
nations, which his delegation championed,
had gone the way of Pardee, Friend was,
to say the least, down in the mouth.
After the convention, had adjourned till
the next day, when the remaining offices
were to be filled. Friend was sitting in the
lobby of the Golden Eagle Hotel sadly
cogitating over his disappointments and
wondering if the morrow would bring
further defeats for his county. He was
considering the advisability of deserting
his delegation, over which a "Jonah"
seemed to hover, when he suddenly
espied Dan Burns. Going up to him with
a rueful countenance, and being aware of
the colonel's well-known predilection for
horses, he began to storm the citadel of
his good graces in the following terms:
"Dan, old fellow, I wish you would put
me next to a few good things. Here I
have been backing a lot of 'skates' and
they have proved themselves 'dead ones'
by coming 'absolutely nowhere." Now,
you are on the inside and I would like to
play a few winners in the convention to
morrow. I hate to be a loser on every
race. Come, now, let me have some tips
on to-morrow's 'events.' "
Burns listened to the entreaty with
out moving a muscle of his countenance
and with a sly wink, he said:
" Friend, I can't give you any tips now,
but I tell you what I will do. I'll go up
stairs and study the 'form book' and I
promise to give you some 'good things'
early to-morrow morning." And with that
the astute colonel vanished up the stairs.

STOLE BREAD AND MILK.
Sentence of Lawrence Stodolski, a
"Wealthy Old Miser, Postponed.
Lawrence Stodolski,' the wealthy old
miser who was arrested about ten days
ago for stealing milk and bread from his
tenants on Natoma street, was again ar
rested yesterday on a similar charge.
The complaining witness is Martin Bian
chi, a milkman, living at 716 Greenwich
street.
When the first case against Stodolski
was called in Judge Low's court yesterday
his attorney, F. C. Castlehun, asked for
a postponement of eentence for a week
as he wanted to show that the old man
had been acting queerly for some time
and was not responsible for his actions.
The Judge granted the request.
Death of Gteorge Hetzel.
George Hetzel, a musician and a mem
ber of the racetrack band at Ingleside,
died suddenly yesterday morning while
conversing with his wife.
He awoke early in the morning and
complained to his wife that he had been
kept awake by severe pains in the region
of the heart.
While he was telling her about it he
was seized with a sudden spasm and died
instantly. Only a few days ago a physi
cian had advised him to quit smoking,
but he failed to heed the advice. An in
quest will be held.

John Baptlsta of Alameda has filed pa
pers in bankruptcy In the United States
District Court. He owes $1429 95 and has
no assets.
WILL SOON CONTEST
ADOLPH SUTRO'S WILL
MRS. KLUGE SUTRO READY TO
OPEN THE FIGHT.
Her Petition to Be Appointed Guar
dian of Her Children Granted
by Judge Troutt.
Mrs. Clara Kluge-Sutro was armed with
authority by Judge Troutt yesterday to
prosecute any action she may see fit on
behalf of her children against the estate
of the late Adolph Sutro. Mrs. Kluge-
Sutro was granted her application to be
apointed guardian of her children, Adolph
and Adolphine, whom she avers are the
children of the deceased philanthropist.
The petition, was granted without oppo
sition. Mrs. Kluge-Sutro took the stand
and simply testified that she was the
mother of Adolph and Adolphine Sutro,
as she called them. No testimony us
to their paternity was offered; this will
be brought out in the contest of the will
of the dead millionaire soon to be insti
tuted.
In the petition Mrs. Kluge-Sutro avers
that the children named have large prop
erty interests within the jurisdiction of
the court. The children, it is said,
through a conveyance made by Sutro,
when he was supporting them and their
mother in the home he built for their oc
cupation at Steiner and Clay streets, are
at present possessed of block No. 219, out
side lands, which is valued at about $20,
000.
The petitioner seeks to prove that she
married Adolph Sutro by contract after
the death of his first wife, Leah. Should
she fail in her efforts to establish this al
legation she hopes to establish beyond the
peradventure of a doubt that her chil
dren are those of Sutro, and thereby gain
for them a share of the rich estate.

Official Count Progresses.
The official count of the ballots cast at
the late election is progressing slowly
and without the discovery of any impor
tant errors. The election officers of the
second precinct of the Twenty ninth Dis
trict were cited to appear to-day ana ex
plain why they had not signe'i the re
turns of the vote on State offic3rs cast
in their precaincts.

Two of the vessels that formed part
of "the regatta" that was held off the
Horn the Ist and 2d of September last
arrived in port yesterday after an excit
ing race.
The four-masted British ship Falls of
Clyde left London and the three-masted
British ship Merioneth left Swansea for
San Francisco within five days of each
ether. On July 4 they were in company,
and from that time until they met off the
Golden Gate they have been playing at
hide and seek. First one and then the
other ship would get the breeze, draw
ahead and disappear for a couple of
weeks or a month, only to find themselves
together again with the first lust and vk-e
versa.
The two vessels were nearly a month
out before they met, and parted after
exchanging signals. As the Merioneth
holds the record for the run between
sea and Shi; Francisco, and was then
days ahead Of the Falls of Clyde,
Captain Rowland naturally expected to
1 San Francisco first, and signaled
Captain Anderson that he would
him on arrival. Anderson signaled back,
"Thank you; I'll do the same for you,"
and the race began. On July 14 the ships

DR. GARDNER'S HANDS
UNTIED AT LAST
There was consternation in the ranks of the Society for the Im
portation of Chinese Women when the news was received from
Washington yesterday that the Secretary of the Treasury had pro
moted Chinese Interpreter Rev. Dr. John Endicott Gardner to the po
sition of Chinese Inspector. It came with the suddenness of a bomb
shell, and before the coolie brokers and their friends in the Federal
building could realize that the impossible had happened, Dr. Gardner
had called upon Customs Collector Jackson and had taken the oath
of office.
The donor was not promoted at the request of Chief Meredith
of the Chinese Bureau. The promotion came about In consequence
of a recommendation made by Special Agtnt Moore in a recent re
port, in which he laid before the Secretary of the Treasury the dif
ficulties under which Dr. Gardner had been laboring, both before and
since Mr. Meredith took charge of the bureau. It has been common
knowledge that the doctor had never been extended the facilities
which should have been afforded him for the suppression of the Chi
nese trade in female slaves. He was used as a phonograph, and his
hands were practically tied behind his back. The doctor will be en
abled to make investieations on his own account, as well as inter
pret, and he will bÂ« no longer hampered by th>- Chinese v ing which
seems to dominate the machinery of exclusion.
Dr. Gardner has a faithful partner In John Lynch, gn Inspector
who has earned his laurels by hard and conscientious work.
eistid of .a. niiOisra- ooe^ajst race.
The four-masted ship Falls of Clyde and the ship Merioneth, bound for San Francisco, were in company on
July 4 last. They met again a fortnight later and once more in the latter part of August. They rounded the Horn
together and fought their way through the same kind of weather to the Farallones, where they found themselves
in company once more. Both took tugs, but the Falls of Clyde beat the Merioneth to an anchorage by a ship's
length. Counting from the time the vessels left England the Merioneth wins by five days, but counting from July
4, when they first met, the Falls of Clyde "won by a nose."

Civil Service Examination.
The United States Civil Service Com
mission announces that on Decmber 14,
15 and 16 an examination will be held In
this city for the position of draughtsman
for marine engines and machinery. The ex
amination will be open to all citizens of
the United States. Persons desiring to
take the examination should communicate
with Miss Belle C. Lewald, secretary- of
the board, at the main postofflce in this
city.
SHOW CASE
ROBBERS ARE
NOW RAMPANT
Market-Street Stores*
Visited Nightly.
POLICE SPECIALLY DETAILED
FOUR ARRESTS MADE WITHIN
THE PAST TEN DAYS.
The Police Object to a Charge of
Burglary Being Arbitrarily
Reduced to Malicious
Mischief.
Complaints are being made almost daily
by storekeepers on Market street to the
police of their showcases being broken
About 1 o'clock yesterday morning a
showcase at the entrance to the Baldwin
clothing store was broken open and sev
eral pairs of gloves were stolen. The
thief broke off the lock, evidently being
afraid that breaking the glass might be
heard. He was observed by Policemen
C. Muphy and R. McDowell and they saw
him making for the Emporium. They
placed him under arrest and found the
gloves on him.
On November 10 a showcase In front of
Maginn's furnishing store, 840 Market
street, was smashed and a large number
of ladies' assorted neckties stolen. No
vember 11 a showcase at the entrance to
the store of the J. M. Brunswick Billiard
Supply Co. was broken and twenty ivory
billiard balls were taken.
In the early morning of November 6 a
showcase in front of Paul Lot's photo
graph gallery was broken open and a
gold watch case was stolen. Policemen
Meyers and McNamara saw three boys
running away from the gallery and they
pursued and captured them. A diamond
which had evidently been used to cut
the glass in the showcase was later found
on the sidewalk in front of the gallery,
and It was assumed that the thieves had
thrown it away when they saw the two
policemen.
The cases were assigned to Judge Low's
court, and at the preliminary examina
tion Monday the Judge, after hearing the
evidence, decided that the charge of bur
glary could not stand and ordered that a
chnrpe of malicious mischief should be
brought against the defendants. They
were booked on that charge; and when
the cases were called yesterday they were
continued until to-day.
The police are dissatisfied with the ac
tion of the Judge, and contend that the
defendants were guilty of burglary or
nothing.

Silver unchanged.

Barley firmer.

Hay steady and Bran weak.

Pouitry !n fair demand. Game unchanged.

Xo change in Wool.

Merchandise quotations about the same.

EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK. Nov. I."..â The premonitory
symptoms of reactions which were noted yes
terday in the stock market and even more
prominently in the bond market found their
Justification to-day. There were periods of
resistance to the decline and individual stocks !
in which the resistance was successful. But
the reaction gained in force as the day pro
pressed and the closing was decidedly heavy
In tone and at (tie low point, net decline being
the rule and reaching as much as a point in
a few stocks. The specialties were active all
day and the movements in them were exceed
ingly various and conflicting and influ<uiced by
individual causes. The market for railroad
showed a heavy tone from the opening,
especially in the internationals, which had the
burden of selling of foreign account to carry, i
The Grangers and Pacific* also encountered ob- j
Etacles to an advance.
The Atchta in Issues continued to be a strong
rallying point for the bull faction, the pre
1 erred stocks rising to 43V Further effort was
marie to sustain the railroad list bidding up
prices for the low priced, stocks, especially
Hi- isp in the southwest. The mystery surround
ing the pending disposition of the Chicago and
Alton also imparted strength to different rail
road stocks on rumors in connection with that
transact ion. Then: were unexplained advances
:n Southern Pacific and of 9 full points
in Chicago and Northwestern preferred. In the
specialties there were prevailing heaviness on
one hand in Sugar, Manhattan and People's
Gas. while on the other hand Metropolitan and
the paper stocks showed marked strength. The
Federal ks have been dealt In hereto
fore as deliverable when issued, a transaction
which did not call for cash. The certificates
are now being Issued and the resulting re
quirement for funds is assigned as the reason
for the weakness of these stocks. Manhattan's
weakness was attributable to a study of the
quarterly statement and the balance sheet.
Other iron and *teel stocks were heavy In sym
pathy. The hardening tendency of the money
market was reflected in the growing heaviness
of bonds, the market for which continued very
active. Total sales, 5C.310.000.
United States new 4s and the F,s declined Vi
and the 3s and the old 4s in the bid price.

THE LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, Nov. 15.â The Evening Post's
financial cablegram says:
The stock markets here were Irregular to-day
and ' less firm. Americans opened below the
parity, operators here being timid concerning
the outcome of the Peace Commission's discus
sion of the Philippine question. Later prices
rallied considerably, but the close was dull
again. Except for nervousness over the Philip
pines the feeling here is very confident in favor
of Americans, as it is felt that the currency
bogey, so much dreaded by English Investors,
has been thoroughly laid. ' ; ;V; V
For a time Grand Trunk was good to-day on
Montreal buying. Spanish 4s were bought
from Paris.
The German demand for gold Is strong at
77s 9%d. The tightness of money in Berlin con
tinues and a further rise in the German bank
rate is quite possible. There was a sharp fall
In Spanish exchange to-day.

CHK'AGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO, Nor. 15.â 1t was evident at th"
opening in Wheat that traders playing for
higher prices were going to have a hard time.
Liverpool, instead of responding to yesterday's
advance, showed a decided inclination to go
the other way, and in this was jir-t alone,
Paris also showing weakness. Liverpool was
led higher to %d lower and Paris 10 centimes
lower. May opened %<B-\i,c lower at 6S',Â«i f rj66 I ric,
and December started lower at 66%QM%a
There were very few buyers of either option for
some time, while selling orders were large,
especially in December, and prices were stead
ily forced down, the first hour's trading re
sulting in May declining to 66 and December
to'66Vic. Besides the weakness of cables, bulls
had to contend with remarkably heavy North
west receipts, prospects of a continuance of
the same on account of favorable weather and
reports from New York that many of yester
day's heavy buyers had turned sellers. All
this was an incentive to short selling. Minne
apolis and Duluth reported 2088 cars to-day.
About 11 o'clock some improvement In prices
took place on the sale of 300,000 bushels cash
Wheat here and the reported sale at New York
of 125 boat loads for export. This caused liqui
dation to stop more or less and started cover
ing by the more timid shorts. December had
risen to 66% c by noon and May had recovered
to 66Â»4c. Bradstreet's statement of the world's
visible, however, threw a damper over the
market. This showed an Increase of 6,723.000
bushels. The figures were much larger than
had been anticipated and the market at once
became weak. Liquidation and short selling
became quite heavy again. During the last
hour's trading prices slowly but surely de
clined May closed at 6Cf/66Vic and December
at 66%e.
Corn developed considerable weakness. There
was heavy liquidation by longs induced by
lower cables fine weather West and the break
in Wheat. Short selling was general. May
closed V4<s%c lower.
Oats were relatively strong. Cash prices were
at a nremlum over futures and an excellent
demand was reported. This was a big help to
the speculative market, and though there was a
*mall decline early with Corn, this was soon
recovered. May closed unchanged.
Provisions were dull and heavy. The market
felt the big receipts of hogs and the weakness
in Corn. Late in the day the market improved
somewhat. January pork closed 2' 2 c lower,
lard 2 J 2 c lower and ribs 2^@sc lower.
The leading futures ranged as follows:

LOW PRICES FOR GRAPES.
NEW YORK. Nov. 15. â The Journal of Com
merce says: Notwithstanding short supplies
speculative interest in Almeria grapes seems
to be almost wholly lacking, which is attrib-
uted to the unsuccessful results of ventures
in this direction since California became an im
portant factor in the supply of good keeping
grapes for holiday and winter trade. Prices,
while rather high on Almeria fruit this season,
are still below ccr Ures obtained In the New
York market at this time In several previous
years. Liverpool is still a factor, so far as
shipments to this side are concerned, and It
will depend In great measure upon how much
she can ship to this market whether prices
will be maintained at their present level or not.
Hut for California competition it is said there
would be no doubt on that score.

HIDES, TALLOW. WOOL AND HOPS.
Hides are easy and the trade are rather look
ing for still lower prices. Hops are selling
briskly at firm prices, mostly at about 14c,
a ethe upper, qualities are scarce. :
A local circular says of Wool: . "Locally there
Is but little to report; but what little there Is
Is more hopeful of a brisk business later along.
One sale of a hundred and * fifty thousand
pounds has been made here, . chiefly bright,
well-grown toothill woo!, at very low cost.
No other local trade. In the East large sales
have recently be*n made at lower prices. The
market there. as well as here Is glutted with
wool. Manufacturers are aware of this, and
therefore take their own time and make their
own terms as to prices, but a good winter trade
in ' manufactured goods will change conditions
to that extent that business after January
ehould be better all around."

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographlc
Office, located in the Merchants' Exchange, Is
maintained in San Francisco for the benef!-. of
niariners without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially invited to vis!? the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street. Ib hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon, 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory. Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any, is published
in the morning papers the following day.
W. S. HUGHES.
Lieutenant, U. S. ft., in charge.

yciTE- In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are given In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign fâ ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the ilepth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the lower low w.xters.

TOO MUCH OF
MOTHER-IN-LAW
MADE TROUBLE
Prof. Depue Is Denied
a Divorce.
WIFE CLUNG TO HER MOTHER
HUSBAND'S EVIDENCE DID NOT
PBOVE DESEBTION.
May E. White Sues Her Husband, Ex-
Rev. Mr. White, for a Divorce,
and Says That He Left
Her.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Nov. 15.
Judge Ogden to-day denied a decree of
divorce in the suit of Professor James F.
Depue against Grace M. Depue, which
â was brought on the ground of desertion.
The main facts of the troubles have
heretofore been published, but to-day the
plaintiff went on the stand and gave ex
pression to additional grievances. His
Ptory was to the effect that there had
been too much mother-in-law, who, wit
ness said, "positively refused to keep out
of my affairs."
"My wife knows that I had money in
the bank," continued the husband, "when
we were married, and that it was our
intention to build a cozy home. But no
sooner had we moved than the mother
came upon the scene in the drama and
attempted to commit suicide because nor
daughter was going to leave her. I have
first and last fought that woman's per
sistent meddling In our affairs, and she
has been the cause of all our trouble."
Speaking of .the sniriting away of his
thirteen-year-old son. witness said the
wife had done this. She had placed the
boy with a Chinaman on a farm In -the
country', the mother forbidding him to
write to her, lest the father learn of his
whereabouts. All this, witness thought,
had been done to bring him Qplaintiffj to
terms.
Judge Ogden, in summing up the ease,
held that a husband's testimony Is not
sufficient to prove desertion; and though
the wife should have fors.uun her par
ents, it had not been proved that she did
not do 80.
A divorce suit was also commenced to
day by May E. White for ;i separation
Fred M. White on the ground
;i. Defendant is a retired minister
of the gospel, and the parties have two
children.
Judge Ogden to-day granted Martha E.
Hand a divorce from B. Frank Hand on
the gTound of extreme cruelty. Plaintiff
iwarded tW monthly alimony, $100
counsel fees and is permitted to resume
her maiden name â Martha K. Taylor.
Default has been entered in the divorce
suit of Rebecca B. Bonln against lsrat-1
â¢ ; ti for failure of defendant to an
swer or demur.

"Gee- whiz! Do you mean to tell me that that namby-pamby flat, In-

"Let me repeat it. Postum Is a crisp, delicious drink.
"Do you understand my language? Postum is good, toothsomÂ© and sat-
isfying.
"But when you serve it half boiled It tastes "Wow! take it away.
"How to make it? Perfectly simple plan. Boil it! Boil It!! 801 l it! I!
"The flavor cannot be brought out of the grains until it has actively
boiled full fifteen minutes. Â»-.
"Excuse me, my dear, you did not boil that Postum fifteen minutes.
You may have had the pot on the stove that long, but that won't do. When
actual bubbling and boiling begins take account of your time, and then
allow full fifteen minutes for genuine boiling.
"Use four heaping teaepdons of Postum to the pint of water, and when
it has properly boiled you have something to be proud of.
"^heri a man wants Postum, he wants it. to taste good.
"There is one sure and unfailing test of the truthfulness of the cook.
If the Postum is , good she has properly boiled it, if it is flat she has not,
and no excuse will change that fact.
"One cur of Postum Food Coffee made right makes one a friend forever.
"If you have been poorly served, try again our way. You will not be dis-
appointed." ... -
If your -feelings don't give way to medicines, try leaving off "offee a
while. You may be one of that great army of active, bright, highly organ-
ized Americans that coffee acts upon as a slow, sure and persistent poison,
that first affects stomach and heart, then liver, bowels, nerves, weak eyes,
etc., etc., finally drifting into paralysis, cancer of stomach, heart disease,
'or some kindred evil. '
Some people seem to drink coffee and whisky without ill effects, but
when a man or woman finds that incipient disease persists in hanging about
them it seems, the part of wisdom to look to the cause. : Thousands of
brainy Americans have found that cause right in their beloved and unsus-
pected coffee cup.
A trial of ten days to a month without coffee but with Postum Food
Coffee (properly made) in its place will tell the tale, and if you find your
troubles gone, your eye bright and step elastic, with a feeling of energy suf-
ficient to do your daily work and enjoy life, you may be sure you have
spotted the worm that has been "hidden under the. bark."

NOTHING TO
BE SECURED
BY RECOUNTS
Church Gains in the
Official Count.
HONEST ELECTION CLERKS
DID THEIR WORK FAITHFULLY
AND WELL.
In One-Half the Returns Jordan
Loses Nine Votes and Stod
dard Fourâ The Sixth
Ward Reversed.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Nov. 15.
As the official count of the returns of
the recent election progresses it is evi
dent that there are no changes to be
made in the figures returned by the news
paper bureau. The tally sheet and fig
uring of over half the county already
canvassed prove that the election officers
did their work very intelligently and that
the Supervisors appointed efficient boards
of election.
The closest votes were those of Frank
Jordan and T. C. Stoddard for County
Clerk. When 10,000 votes (about half) had
been counted, embracing the whole of the
city of Oakland, Jordan had lost nine and
Stoddard four from the returns credited
to them by the bureau. As Jordan has
a majority of over 450 the official count
will 'fiot improve his position in the small
est degree.
Supervisor Church, It now appears, car
ried the Sixth Ward. The unofficial count
from this ward credited Church with 8M
votes to Glassock's 60Â«5. The official count
adds five votes to Mr. Church's figures,
thus giving him the ward by three votes.
The vote on the other candidates does
not vary at all. There has been some
talk of a probable contest in the Glas
cock-Church district, but the work of
the election officers is proved to be such
that there is little to be hoped for from
a recount, as Church's majority now
stands at 116.
The preparations for a great ratifica
tion next Saturday night embrace a
programme that will do Justice to the po
sition AJameda County occupies in the
Republican party. Marching clubs from
several cities will take part in the dem
onstration the city will be illuminated on
its principal streets, and after the par
ade there will be a royal time at the Mac
(l,iii..ugh Theater. The State banner will
be displayed on the stage and before It
this county will renew her allegiance to
the party which has recently scored such
successes. ._*.*Â»
It has been announced that Cxovemor
olect Gage does not think he can be pres
ent but Hon. Jacob Neff and many other
successful candidates on the State and
county tickets will be here and join in
the â¢Jollification.
Mi-, ady a candidate has come forward
tncing his desire for city honors at
the next municipal election. This pioneer
of what will no doubt prove to be an
army of office-seekers is D. W. Alden of
the Third Ward, who would like to be a i
Councilman.
District Attorney-elect John J. Allen
was to-day appointed a deputy District j
Attorney by District Attorney Snook. Mr.
Allen will get no pay. but will be given i
the privilege of two months' apprentice
ship in the office he will fill on the new
year.

Dr. Chapman presented a glowing re
port of the work of tTTfe league during the
past year. The doctor haa always Deen
known as a good man for taking up col
lections, ;md his begging propensities have
been frequently referred to. His report
showed that he fully maintained his repu
tation, for during the year he has held
seventy-six meetings, collecting the sum
of $9243 87, an average of $121 77 at each
meeting. Dr. Chapman believed that the
day is not far off when the league will be
strong enough to demand the closing of
all saloons. His report favored submit
ting the question as a whole to the vot-
ers.
Rev. T. C. McChe*ney. president of the
Anti-Saloon League of Santa Clara., said
that the work would be a great deal more
effective if the branch leagues had acted
more Independently of the State league. He
believed that if each league made a more
thorough campaign in its district that
practical prohibition would soon become
a feasible proposition.

SEIBEN KNEW SLATER
WAS IN ARREARS
IP MRS. SLATER HAD PAID ALL
WOULD HAVE BEEN WELL.
Secret Compact Between the Two
Financial Officers of the
Lodge.
ALAMEDA, Nov. 15.â Alameda Lodge
No. 5, A. O. U. W., has under investiga
tion the acts of two of its officers in con
nection with the $2000 certificate en tho
life of the late Dr. Thomas Slater. The
story published in this morning's Call
connecting the names of Philip Seiben and
O. S. Meysel with alleged peculiar tran
sactions with regard to the late doctor's
life insurance created a sensation in fra
ternal circles and was a particular sub
ject of comment among the members of
the A. O. U. W.
Investigation shows that Meysel had no
hand whatever in the deal. Seiben, in an
Interview this evening, frankly admitted
that he knew Slater was in arrears to the
lodge at the time of his death: that he
had conspired with the ex-financier, R. H.
Ohea, to keep the fact from the Grand
Lodge, and that he would not have "let
the cat out of the bag" if he had not
been accused of blackmail.
The doctor's assessment was due June
28. His lodge met the following evening.
R. H. Ohea was the receiver and it was
his duty to report delinquent members.
According to Mr. Seiben's statement he
did not do It.
"Ohea whispered to me," said Seiben,
"that Slater was in arrears. rWe both
sympathized with him and his wife, and
agreed among - ourselves to say nothing
about it. July 5 we received word from
Redding that he had died. We did not
know what to do about his life insurance,
for we knew he had not paid his assess
ments. We â ' felt eorry for the widow
and again agreed to say nothing. Our
lodge met July -6. Ohea went out of of
fice and Meysel ' took his place. Ohea
turned over to his successor money for
Slater's June and July dues. When ? our
lodge made Its retorqs to the Grand. Lodge
HAS ASCENDED THE BENCH.
OAKLAND, Nov. 15.â A. A. Moore Jr.
was appointed Police Judge by the Mayor
this morning and at once assumed the du
Â£ his office. Mr. Moore has been a
Deputy District Attorney for some years
and has been Prosecuting Attorney of the
Police Court. Judge Moore's first act was
Slater's assessments were included and
everything was all right.
"The first time Mrs. Slater saw me af
ter the death of her husband she said
she would pay me the money as soon as
she received her insurance. I had a note
against her husband for J350, but told her
if she would give me an order on the
Grand Lodge for $250 I would deduct $100.
She agreed to this and the order whs
Bigned and sealed before a notary public.
"When it was found the money was in
two checks they charged me with black
mail. Then I turned to the grand officer
who was standing by and told him that
the doctor's assessments had not been
paid on time. That stopped the whole
business. I will admit that if she had
paid me I would not have said anything
about her husband's delinquency."
MARSTON CAMPBELL
UNDER A HOT FIRE
SCHMIDT'S NEW COMPANY IS A
PUZZLEB.
Supervisors Are to Examine the
Books of the Bridge-Builders
To-Morrow.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Nov. 15.
The intricacies of bridge building and
the composition of corporations were
delved into by the Supervisors and sev
eral attorneys to-day. A keen battle has
developed between the two contractors
who would build the new massive steel
drawbridge across the estuary for the
county. The Schmidt Construction Com
pany did not take its books into the
board-room to-day, and Attorneys If. C.
Chapman and W. R. Davis appeared for
Schmidt and Cotton Bros, respectively.
During the arguments Attorney Emil
Nuabaumer reinforced M. Davis in the
i pi-s-fxamination of Schmidt, and at the
close of the day the company was told to
bring its books into the board-room so
that the Supervisors can satisfy them
selves as to its standing.
The contract is one of great importance
to the county, as it involves the expendi
ture of probably not lf-s? than $100,000. The
bid of the Schmidt Construction Company
is the lowest, but the Supervisors do not
believe, apparently, that tne company has
the necessary plant or experience to erect
such a bridge.
Maraton Campbell, the engineer of the
company, stated on the witness-stand
that the bidders had purchased from the
firm of Peterson & Johnson a plant mor
oughly capable of building the bridge. He
exhibited a bill of salt- showing that. $0500
had been paid for the plant.
Engineer Le Conte of Berkeley was put
on the stand to testify as to the standing
of the company and as to his experience
with it in Berkeley. Mr. Le Conte was not '
permitted to give much information. ;i.s
Schmidt's attorney stated that the whole j
thir.g was simply a play t>> draw out tes
timony in favor of Cotton Brothers and!
to belittle the Schmidt Construction Com- I
pany. and that the testimony if permitted
would be circulated all over the coast by
the Cotton Brothers to the detriment of
Schmidt. To-morrow morning the books i
of the Schmidt company will be examined, i
but it Is quite evident that the board will !
either award the bid to Cotton Brothers
or decide to reject all bids and readver
tise.

ALMOST A FATAL ERROR.
A. Vici Enters the Wrong House and
Is Slashed With a Razor.
OAKLAND, Nov. 15.â Antonio Vici, a
tinker, came within a very narrow margin
of being murdered last night. Vici lives
on Fourth street, and as he was going
home he unintentionally entered the
house next door to his. In the hall he
was met by a burly negro who whipped
out a razor and slashed him. cutting an
ugly, though not serious, gash over the
left eye.
The injured man made good his escape
without further bloodshed or damage, and
his wound was soon dressed and sewed.
When Officers Sherry and Hvnes and
Soecial Officer Doolan arrived at the
house of the negro the latter had disap
peared At least he could not be found
and Vici admitted that he could not iden
tify him.

THREE CHOICES IN EVIDENCE
LOST GIRL WAS AGAIN OVER
LOOKED BY THE MAJORITY.
Plan Was Unable to Get Inside the
Honey â Obsidian Enjoyed a
Gallop â Una Colorado
Won Driving.
The horses ran well up to form at
Irgleside yesterday, but the crowd seemed
loath to separate themselves from their
roin. for the play was exceedingly light.
Obsidian. I'r.a Colorado and San Venado.
all stanchly backed choices, won for the
talent. The victory of Rey del Tierra over
his field did not create much of a furor,
but Lost Girl, which defeated a fast
bunch of speed in the fourth event at odds
Of S to 1. was slightly overlooked.
The attendance was nearly up to the
average, but the layers of odds all com
plain of a lack of business. Unless some
of the Lantern importations are trotted
cut era long, the game will be in need
01" repairs.
Piggott started in the day well by pilot-
Ing Obsidian in winner of the opening
seven furlong jaunt, going into the start
er's hands a 7 to 10 favorite. The brown
colt led all the way and won galloping
from Casdale, a3oto 1 shot. Ocorona beat
the heavily played Oraibee for the show.
The Joe Hooker gelding, Una Colorado,
looked far and away the best of the col
lect.on in the six furlong sprint follow
ing, but was hard ridden at the finish to
beat Eeina de Los Angeles half a length.
Jack o' Lantern made his first start in
many moons, finishing a fair third. He
phould do later on.
For the mile and a furlong run a warm
favorite was furnished in Plan, sent to
the post a 4 to 5 proposition. When the
chestnut gelding could not shake off Rev
del Tierra he stopped badly, finally finish
ing fourth. Rey del Tierra had things his
own way, and "led the 40 to 1 chance Dr.
Bernays over the line with plenty in
reserve. Benamela found the game too
fast for him, but managed to capture
Bhow honors.
The crowd fell heavily over the result
of the six furlong spin, fourth on the
card. Piggott had already ridden three
winners and his mount Judge Stouffer
was plunged on, at one time being an
even money chance. The Judge headed
his company to the stretch, but there
found the pace too warm. Near the pad
dock it looked to lie between Toribio and
Little Alarm, when Bullman brought Lost
Girl up on the outside, beating the former
sprinter handily by a length. Zamar was
well supported, but was never dangerous.
The Baldwin colt San Venado at last
won a purse, but appears many pounds
below a real good one. He closed a 9 to
10 favorite, and when he led the outsider
Bonnie lone past the judges two lengths,
was being urged along by Rutter. Colo
nel Dan, the second choice, was pulled up
by Piggott at the start.
Track Notes.
Starter Caldwell fined Joe Piggott, who
rode Colonel Dan in the last event, $25
for pulling up and spoiling- the start.
Piggott claimed the horse bore in toward
the rail. He was reprimanded and warn
ed by the judges to ride his mounts out
In future, whether the start suited him or
not. Joe probably overlooked the fact
that the recall flag is not in vogue at
Ingleside.
Bullman was originally carded to ride
Rey del Tierra, but at the suggestion of
Presiding Judge Smith, Owner Murray
was prevailed upon to mount Piggott. It
apparently did not suit in some quarters,
but the change was undoubtedly a wise
one.

afternoon at the races. He "doped" Lost

COLLAPSE OF A SCAFFOLD.
One Man Killed and Two Badly In-
jured at San Mateo.
SAN MATEO, Nov. 15.â While workmen
we're engaged yesterday on a brick build
ing about thirty feet from the ground
the scaffolding on which they mood gave
way and all were thrown to the ground.
Their names were R. E. Nangle of San
Jose, Herman Pils'ter and Spencer Good
win of San Francisco. Herman Pilster
died an hour after the accident. The
other two men were badly injured, but it
is believed they will recover. Dr. Curnow
of San Jose and Drs. Thompson and
Baldwin of San Mateo are attending
them, while they are still at the San
Mateo Hospital. Herman Pilster leaves a
widow and four children unprovided for.
Suicide of a Chinaman.
Ah Hoo, the servant of Mrs. S. Cohn of
419 Van Ness avenue, was found dead in
his bed early yesterday morning with the
sis turned on. It is supposed to be a case
of suicide.
Asthma, bronchitis, catarrh cure jruarant'd. Dr.
Gordin, Sanitarium. 514 Pine, nr. Kearny, S.F. Cai.
THE SAN FRAKCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, yOYEMBER If?, 1898.
ENGINES OF
THE PETERS
WERE ALONE
Engineer Ran to Them
Just Before the Col
lision.
CZARINA BLEW FIRST BLAST
HER HEAD AND SIDE LIGHTS
WERE BURNING.
First Officer Kitts a Positive Witness.
The Biver Boat's Signals Were
Confusing.
The official investigation into the Pe
ters-Czarina disaster was resumed yester
day morning before Inspectors of Hulls
and Boilers Bolles and Bulger. The ex
amination of the first witness, J. H.
Deare. assistant engineer of the Peters,
brought out the interesting fact that
there was no one at the throttle at the
critical moment when her bells rang to
stop and back. Deare testified that he
was In charge and the engines were alone
at the time. He was standing in the
door of the engine room and immediately
went to them on the run. After the colli
sion he did not leave the engine room
until the water came up to his knees. He
thought if the Peters was going full
speed ahead she could be stopped and
start going astern in a minute and a half.
William Bell, a laborer on Angel Island,
heard two whistles and then another. He
saw a headlight but did not know which
vessel carried it.
John Crowley, nlgrht watchman of the
Peters, said that he was on the lookout
for passing vessels and saw the Czarina
; ahead before the whistles blew, but did
not report the circumstance, thinking 1 the
men in the pilot house, having- a better
| view, would see her also.
! A. W. Dennisson, steward of the Peters,
I testified in regard to the efforts made to
1 save the passengers, and that they were
! nearly all provided with life preservers.
Bernard Xyberg, a passenger or. the
Peters with some nautical knowledge,
i said he was standing on the right side of
the freignt deck and he first saw a red
light dead ahead. The Peters had not fln
i ished giving two signals when the Czar
ina gave one and he thought It peculiar.
Between the two whistles he saw the
green light, and after stepping to the
I starboard of the Peters he saw both
â  lights, but did not look for a headlight.
Chief Engineer Gustav Holtz of the
j Czarina eakl his vessel blew one whistle
and the Peters one, then the latter blew
two and the Czarina again answered with
one. His steamer was stopped, reversed
i but her headway was not checked.
At the afternoon session May Beckett,
a Salvation Army lassie, who was a pas
j senger on the Peters, said that she heard
â¢ only one set of signals, two from the Pe
ters and one from the approaching
steamer.
Edward Kitts, first officer of the Czar- I
ma, made a good witness and the most
rigid cross examination failed to shake
his positive testimony. He testified as to
the whistles, saying that the Czarina be
gan with one blast. The wheel was shift
ed to port and the Czarina swung to star
board, but how much he could not tell.
Cnptaln Seaman did not order it changed.
The one whistle was given as soon as he
saw the side lights of the Peters and so
reported to his captain. According to the i
standing rule on the Czarina, when an- ;
other vessel is approaching, he looked at
his own side lights and headlight and saw I
that they were burning and in their prop
er places.
In reply to a question by H. J. Corcoran
of the California Navigation Company,
he denied in the most emphatic terms :
that the headlight was not burning at the
time, but had oeen lowered, lighted and ;
hoisted again In place after the Peters j
was sighted.
Corcoran desired to prove that Kitts
was in charge of the Czarina and that he
did not have a license to navigato the ;
waters of the bay, but this contention
wns set at rest when the record was pro
duced, and Inspector Bolles said that :
Kitts' license entitled him to act as chief !
mate in any waters on any vessel.
Furthermore, in reply to a direct ques- .
tion Kitts faid that Captain Seaman was
In charge of the Czarina from the time
she left Vallejo.
Kitts heard no order given by Captain
Seaman to put the wheel to starboard
after it v.as put to port, but it was held
in the latter pofitlon until the order to
go full speed astern, which came about a i
minute before the collision. Before this
the port bow of the Czarina and the star
board bow of the Peters were each on the !
open sea.
G. F. Rave, first officer of the Peters,
was at supper wiien the collision occurred
and testified as to the taking off of the
passe-Tigers. Ho thought the reason why
the six Chinamen had been drowned was
that the flow of water which went art
carried them off thair feet.
Captain Holdswofth of the Peters was
recalled and t=nid he only paw the head
light nf the Czarina when the crash came
and that the Peters holds steady on her
course nnd readily answers her helm.
The investigation will be concluded to
day. Â»____
FIGHT FOR PURE
FOODS HAS BEGUN
DRAFT OF A BLLL CREATING A
SPECIAL COMMISSION.
War Will Bo Waged Against All
Who Make or Sell Adulterated
Goods â Much Power.
An important meeting was held yester
day In the rooms of the Manufacturers'
and Producers' Association by those in
terested in the pure foods movement.
Those present were members of the body
mentioned and of the Health Depart
ment, consisting of J. W. Kerr, A.
Sbarboro, Assistant District Attorney J.
J. Dunne, Dr. J. M. Williams, Secretary
E. Godchaux of the Board of Health, In
spector J. P. Dockery and Dr. Bazet.
Attorney Dunne has prepared the draft
of a bill to be presented to the next Leg
islature, the purpose of which 1r to cre
ate a State pure foods bureau or commis
sion. Mr. Dunne explained that the bill
was drawn in the rough from the Ohio
law. Its purpose is to concentrate the re
sponsibility in the matter of enforcing the
laws relating to this subject. After con
siderable discussion it was decided to
adopt the draft of the bill, which em
bodies the following features:
The proposed commission shall consist
of three members, to be appointed by the
Governor from the northern, central and
southern parts of the State. They shall
devote their entire time to the enforce
ment of the laws relating to foods,
drinks, drugs and unlawful labels. They
shall prosecute all violators of the laws,
and all local District Attorneys and
health officers shall be subordinate to the
commission in matters in which pure
foods, etc.. are concerned. The board
shall have power to enter or invade prem
ises where goods, drugs, etc., are sold and
take samples for inspection or chemical
analysis. All moneys from fines imposed
by courts shall be paid to the commission,
to be in turn delivered over to the State
Treasurer for the general fund of the
State.
In addition to making annual reports to
the Governor, the commission will sug
gest to the Legislature such laws as are
necessary to effect the desired end. The
act creating the commission abolishes all
other commissions whose duties would
conflict with those of the proposed com
mission. The commissioners shall hold
office four, three and two years, accord
ing to their appointment.
It was decided that four employes would
be necessary, a manager, chemist, attor
ney and inspector. The question of ap
propriation was discussed, the conclusion
being; that the commissioners and em
ployes should have $300 a month, and that
the Legislature should appropriate at
least $75,000 for the commission, all sur
plus going back into the general fund.
Inspector JDoc-kery in discussing the sit
uation said that such a commission would
do more good for the State than double
the amount asked for. One of the great
est benefits would be in the confidence of
the whole world in the goods sent from
this State when it becomes generally
known that California sends o^it of its
borders only products of an absolutely
pure quality.
Big Seizure of Fish.
The inmates of the Almshouse will to
day receive a genuine treat in the shape
of some of the finest fish that can be
found in the waters of the river or bay.
The gift comes from the Fish Commis
sioners and consists of 250 pounds of
striped bass. The fishermen around the
bay have for some time past been viola
ting the laws by netting striped bass of
less than three pounds in weight, fish
that when mature run from thirty to
fifty pounds, and which they sold in the
local market.
Deputies Vogelsang and Davis learned
that this lot of fish had been shipped
through Wells. Fargo * Co. from Giant
station in Contra Costa County and
the seizure was made yesterday
morning. The commission concluded
that it would be of no use to
attempt to make any arrests, as
there are a goodly number of tlsh dealers
now before the courts for violating the
law, and it is only folly to clog the court
calendars until convictions have been se
cured.
Death of W. J. Owen.
TV. J. Owen, the well-known capitalist,
passed away on Monday afternoon at his
residence, 1200 Jones street. His death
was very sudden. Early Sunday he com
plained of not feeling well and the indis
position turned into a stroke of paralysis
which brought on unconsciousness. In
this condition he remained until the early
evening of Monday, when he paid thÂ«
debt he owed to nature.
Mr. Owen came to this city in early
days and . engaged in several employ
ments, finally taking up with the butch
ering business. Later he became a part
ner in the firm of Grace & Owen. He re
tired from active life a few years ago,
and since has been living quietly with his
family, which consisted of his wife, son
and daughter. Deceased .was a native of
Missouri and was C 7 years of age. The
funeral, which is to be private, will take
place at 2 o'clock to-day.
A VERDICT WILL BE
RENDERED TO-DAY
JURY IN BECXER-CREEGANCASE
LOCKED UP ALL NIGHT.
Second Trial of the Skillful Forg-ers
Occupied Three Weeks and
Was a Spirited Fight.
Yesterday marked the close of the trial
of Becker and Creegan, and at half-past
five Judge Wallace had finished his
charge and the case went to the jury.
Judge Wallace left for his home at 6
o'clock, leaving 1 instructions to lock the
jury up for the night and announcing
that be would not give the case any fur
ther consideration until 10 o'clock this
morning.
The second trial of tho famous forgery
case occupied three weeks. The criminal
conspiracy by which the Nevada Bank
was defrauded out of $20,000 lias attracted
the attention of the entire banking world,
the reason being, not so much because of
the size of the swindle as because Becker,
who is known to the police the world over
by the sobriquet of "The Dutchman," baa
long enjoyed the reputation of being the
most dangerous furger of bank paper In
the world.
The first trial of the defendants result
ed in a conviction and both were sen
tenced to life imprisonment. There was
abundant rejoicing among the bankers
when the verdict of the jury was an
nouncec but it was short lived, as the Su
preme Court sent the case back for a
new trial because the verdict had been
secured by the uncorroborated testimony
of Frank Seavor and McClusker, accom
plices in the swindle.
His Zither Sounds No More.
Joseph GKchwardner, the well-known
zither player and teacher, died suddenly
of heart failure at Liepnitz's drugstore
on Sutter street yesterday. During his
life Gochwardner h;i* struck the strings
of his instrument in the presence of near
ly all of the crowned hea-ds of Europe,
and on one occasion played for President
Grant. In this city he will be remember
ed by all the prominent club men, having
spent many hours among them when
sweet music was required. The theater
managers sought him and he has enter
tained audiences from nearly every stage
in San Francisco.
He recently visited Portland. Oregon,
but finding that his health was rapidly
failing, returned t<> this city. Deceased
v.us 60 years of age and leaves a widow.
His remains are at the undertaking par
lors of Valenti Marini & Co., 1524 Stockton
street.
Symphony To-Morrow.
The San Francisco Symphony Society
commences its season at the Orpheum on
Thursday at 3:15. The American piece
will be the "Mepomene" overture by
George Whitfield Chadwick, and the
symphony Tschaikowsky's No. 4, a work
as yet unheard in San Francisco. An
other first timer hero Is Bach'F overture
and suite in A minor for strings, with a
solo part for flute Mr. Rodemann will

THE California Debris Commission, having
received applications to mine by the hydrau-
lic process from George W. Allen and E. P.
Thomas, In the Hangman's Gulch mine, near
Placerville, El Dorado County, to deposit
tailings in Hangman's ravine; from Sam B.
Lusk and J. J. Millar, in the Sampson min-
ing claim, in Gold Lake mining district. Si-
erra County, to deposit tailings in a ravine
below the mine; from E. Reynolds and F.
Carter, in the Morrlstown mine, near Port
Wine, Sierra County, to deposit tailings in
west branch of Little Canyon Creek; from
George D. H. Meyers, in the Myers placer
mine, near Placerville, El Dorado County,
to deposit tailings in Johnson's North Can-
yon: and from Frank and Antone Leveroni,
In the Corsica mine, near Sierra City, Sierra
County, to deposit tailings in old pits, gives
notice that a meeting will be held at room
89, Flood building, San Francisco, Cal., on
November 28, 1898, at 1:30 p. m.

ATTEMPTED TO
ASSASSINATE
A SERGEANT
John Nixon Is Made
a Special Target.
MISSED HIM BUT SIX INCHES
JAMES WATSON SUSPECTED AND
ARBESTED.
His Gun Had Been Fired Recently
and Contained a Loaded Car
tridge â He Denies His
Guilt.
A disgruntled private of Battery I of
the Third T'nited States Artillery, sta
tioned at Fort Point, attempted to as
sassinate John Nixon, the first sergeant
of the battery, shortly before 10 o'clock
Monday night- The weapon used wa*
one of the Krag-Jorgensen rifles, with
â¢which the men of the battery are armed,
and the bullet passed -within six inches of
Sergeant Nixon, tho intended mark.
Nixon was fitting at his desk writing,
â when he was startled by the report of a
gun and the crash of a bullet striking
a chest of clothing beside him. He im
mediately ran from the tent, but in the
darkness could not see the marksman.
The call to arms was sounded twice %om
the guard tent as as the shot was
heard, and the entire battery fell Into
line. Sergeant Nixon reported the occur
ence to Lieutenant William Forse. who
la^ln temporary command of the battery,
and on his order all the arms were
stacked and placed under a heavy guard.
Captain Diens, the commander of the
battery, who is on detached duty rmis
tering out the Sixth California Regiment,
was notified of the shooting yesterday
morning and returned to his command.
He. with Lieutenant Forse and Sergeant
Nixon, made a careful examination of the
Ifuns and found two which bore the ap
pearance of having been fired recently.
One of these contained a loaded cartridge
and belongs to Private James Watson and
the other to Private Lewis. Both men
claimed that they had fired their guns
on Sunday. Lewis was able to prove his
assertion, but Watson was unable to bring
satisfactory evidence as to his statement,
and was locked up in the guard house
pending an investigation of the case.
The powder remaining in the barrel of
the gun belonging to Lewis, which is
known to have been discharged on Sun
day, had rusted the barrel slightly and
â was of a brownish color, but that in Wat
son's gun was still black, as if it had
been fired but a short time. This Is the
â¢only evidence which has been found
against Watson, and he protests his in
nocence.
Sergeant Nixon is at a loss to know
why Watson or any one else In the bat
tery should wish to kill him.
"I never had any trouble with Watson,"
said the sergeant, "and always considered
him one of the best friends I had among
the men in the battery. It is true that
I was obliged to place him under arrest
several times for being drunk and dis
orderly, hut he always took it in good
part, seeming to realize that I was only
obeying orders. In not a few cases the
men whom I have had occasion to arrest
have grumbled at me, but of all the times
Watson has been in the guard house he
never made a complaint of my actions
toward him. The position of "first ser
geant is not an enviable one by any
means, as the men often think that when
I place them under arrest it is of my own
volition instead of on account of orders
I have received; but I didn't think any
man under me had such a hatred of me
that he would wish to kill me."
Some of Nixon's friends say that he Is
very popular among the men, but others
In the regiment claim that he is univer
sally disliked. Among the soldiers of the
Sixth California, which is encamped near
Battery 1, the report is that Nixon is
very hard on the men under him, and
they attribute it to the fact that he has
been in the regular army so long and a
first sergeant for so many years that he
has come to think himself the "whole
thing." There is a rumor among the men
employed on the works at Port Point
and among the soldiers of the Sixth that
Nixon had had trouble with W r atson on
the evening of the shooting. This rumor
Is that Watson had a lady friend visit-
Ing him and Dixon hearing of it, ordered
her out of the camp.
The bullet intended for Nixon was fired
from a distance of about fifty yards. It
passed through the bedding of* the ser
geant's cot, struck the leg of an over
turned stool about six inches behind
Nixon, penetrated a chest and the cloth-
Ing in it, and lodged in a sack of woolen
underclothing. The sergeant attributes
his escape to the fact that the would-be
assassin aimed at his shadow. Nixon
â¢was sitting at one corner of his desk
Instead of directly in front of it. The
man with the gun probably supposed that
the sergeant was in front of the desk and
directly between him and the light, so
aimed at the shadow, which, from the
position in which he stood, was half a
foot west of Nixon.
